```
"mario" <mmc_vw1200@hotmail.com> wrote in message 
news:9aed5964-acb0-414b-be71-acd7d8d7a61c@l64g2000hse.googlegroups.com...
>i need it because of a production parameter of iron helices and
>they are cutted in special angles and for those parameters i  need the
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>for the parameters!!

Someone suggested an iterative solution...

PERFORM VARYING X ..
   E = SIN (X)
  IF E CLOSE ENOUGH TARGET
     EXIT PERFORM
  END-IF
END-PERFORM

.. and I would think you should be able to find a series expansion formula 
for arcsin somewhere on the Internet. Try your favorite search engine with 
"ARCSIN SERIES EXPANSON" and take it from there.

(Hell, if you can have EQUAL and GREATER and LESS operators, why not a CLOSE 
ENOUGH?)
```

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-07-28T16:04:39+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6f4uqkF9mi52U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<v6j8k.107357$I42.14426@fe04.news.easynews.com> <a70e5adb-b887-40d9-a829-3a75f63aad88@u36g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <g3tqvv$bjn$1@reader2.panix.com> <6cg94jF3gm8nmU1@mid.individual.net> <r966641piavtlfe3ov1pgbg6oiqll6ojq3@4ax.com> <6chfhvF3gm61nU1@mid.individual.net> <lta86493p3lh3st265vlni5vh72p0tas9a@4ax.com> <6cldqkF3fq5n5U1@mid.individual.net> <vlab645g2aa2idppk6r0n6o3qhsv4nlnk3@4ax.com> <6cm7h2F3ghd83U1@mid.individual.net> <5p5q849u8nb77g3kgflt1bk5u72pbveib5@4ax.com>`

```


"Clark F Morris" <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote in message 
news:5p5q849u8nb77g3kgflt1bk5u72pbveib5@4ax.com...
> On Sat, 28 Jun 2008 19:28:32 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[67 more quoted lines elided]…
> upgraded their development environments?

Yes, that does have a bearing on it. For decades we used ROSCOE, TSO, SPF 
and accepted the way they worked. To be fair, mainframe based applications 
were never going to be very graphical... (Visual). The vendors would argue 
there was no pressure from the user base, who were perfectly happy with what 
they had, the user base could argue that they didn't know any better. The 
result: status quo... for decades.

As tools from companies like MicroFocus and Fujitsu became available, 
developing mainframe applications OFF the mainframe became more viable. It 
freed the system for processing applications (which is why the company 
bought it in the first place) and gave developers a dedicated processor at 
their workstation for developing and debugging. Slowly, but surely, as 
developers became used to working in a Windowed environment they came to 
expect more immediate visual feedback. Ask anyone who has ever used MF 
Animator and Compuware's XPEDITOR which one they prefer (especially for 
intensive COBOL debugging sessions of long duration). Both good tools, but 
the Visual environment wins every time.

Now it is really too late. Even if the IDE were the same, writing thousands 
of lines of hand carved code to develop applications, and then maintaining 
it afterwards on an indefinite, ongoing basis, is no longer financially 
viable for any but the largest corporations and government, (where waste is 
accepted as part of the culture...:-)). It is too labour intensive and the 
labour is getting harder to find and is too expensive. Offshore outsourcing 
looked like it might be a solution for a while, but more and more companies 
are coming to realize that it is really just a finger in the dyke. There are 
other considerations about outsourcing, and even if it was all to work 
perfectly, the end result is not as good as could be obtained with other 
tools and technology. Just like a puppy not being only for Xmas, procedural 
code is for life, and you better be prepared to spend a lot of money on it. 
In the meantime, you aren't going anywhere, just managing to keep it all 
running.

Object technology is a much more viable solution. Not because of fashion, or 
technology, but because it doesn't require the same high levels of 
maintenance, it enables things to be built quickly and deployed easily, and 
it DOESN'T need thousands of lines of code. The TCO on OO systems is a 
fraction of those built in COBOL.(I know what it cost me to build and 
maintain certain applications in COBOL, and I know what it currently costs 
me to build applications that are even more complex, using OO technology... 
the new systems are around 20% of what the old ones cost. And they are 
actually more fun to write... :-))

It is very interesting to me (and I find it admirable) that companies like 
IBM and MicroFocus (not intending to slur others by omission) are taking a 
responsible stance on the current investment in COBOL. Support is being 
continued (at least for now), migration tools and options are being offered 
and there is demonstrated commitment to a user base that represents less 
than 10% of all IT in the world, although it is critical code for many major 
corporations.

(Yes, I know about the billions of lines of COBOL that Gartner claim, but if 
it is running on 3 sites it doesn't mean much... :-) In 1998 there were 
around 20,000 IBM mainframes worldwide; today, the figure is 10,000 (balance 
this against an estimated 173,000,000  other servers that DON'T use 
COBOL...)... nevertheless, IBM are optimistic on the mainframe, and 
investing large amounts to support it and their other products (1.2 billion, 
last year...They are predicting mainframe "growth" to reach 5% this year, 
although there is talk in the company about new products NOT being referred 
to as "mainframes".))

So the COBOL community is not in danger of being abandoned. At least as long 
as they can keep forking out for support and products...:-) But, you have to 
ask, how long can that go on for? Sooner or later, a $20,000,000 budget 
(say) for IT on the balance sheet is going to raise some eyebrows. I know of 
one insurance company (a couple of decades back...) where they saw it and 
said: "We're not an IT company. Why are we spending that amount on IT?" 
They sold their mainframe and all existing software, then leased it back for 
$8,000,000 a year. A few years later they moved to a package, based on 
client server (it might have been SAP, I'm not sure...) and I understand 
their current IT budget, including network support, is around $7,000,000 a 
year.(However, that is hearsay and I can't confirm it.)

So, yes, having a decent IDE for COBOL could have delayed things a bit.

And it is also true that anything that can be done to increase current COBOL 
productivity (thereby lessening that number on the Balance Sheet) will 
extend the use of COBOL on a given site.

But ultimately, unless COBOL was supporting OO it was doomed.

That's the really ironic part. The vendors realised this and put OO into 
COBOL. The community rejected it.

The rest is history.

> When I read about what you
> have available in Visual Studio with C#, I am envious.  I believe that
> IBM is doing the same sort of thing with Websphere only at a cost that
> requires management approvals and research.

They are backing Eclipse and it is a very good tool, but VS 2008 has more 
extensive facilities and features. Eclipse is a quantum leap ahead of SPF, 
for example.

Both Micro Focus and Fujitsu offer Visual Studio (and have integrated COBOL 
into it) with their .NET products, but these are not cheap. (MS offer it 
free, with all of the major .NET languages included). The thought occurred 
to me that it probably wouldn't be too hard to configure VS for COBOL (it is 
designed to support any language) but I think it would be a few days work 
and I simply haven't the time or need at present.


> As a member of the SHARE
> Guide Language Futures Task Force I can say that the environment you
> describe is what we believed was necessary.  What you describe makes
> Endeavor (a source and object program control management system) look
> amateurish.

I have used ENDEAVOR and also PANVALET (which it replaced on many sites). VS 
2008 source control is via a TEAM module (option) that is simple and 
efficient, and allows team members to be situated locally or remotely (or a 
mix of both). No comparison.

Pete.
```

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>

## Thread: RM COBOL Question — 5 this month / 6 total

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers)_

### RM COBOL Question

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-07-03T12:05:11+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6d2jdnFhqkeU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
I was told that RM COBOL cannot randomly access an ISAM file using a 
secondary key, with READ... KEY IS.

Instead, it is necessary to use a START on the secondary key, then issue a 
READ NEXT.

This seems like such a violation of the standard (and so grossly 
inefficient...) that I wondered if anyone using RM COBOL might confirm, deny 
or comment on it?

(It has some important impacts when converting to RDB...)

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: RM COBOL Question

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2008-07-03T03:04:15+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<PkXak.48262$kx.3296@pd7urf3no>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<6d2jdnFhqkeU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<6d2jdnFhqkeU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> I was told that RM COBOL cannot randomly access an ISAM file using a 
> secondary key, with READ... KEY IS.
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete.

Methinks, somebody is feeding you a load of guff !

----------------------------------------------------------
The Indexed File Control Entry :
---------------------------------
SELECT file-name
ASSIGN TO RANDOM 	("external-file-name")
			(data-name-1)
RESERVE integer(AREA/AREAS)
ORGANIZATION is INDEXED
ACCESS MODE IS (SEQUENTIAL/RANDOM/DYNAMIC)
RECORD KEY IS data-name-2
ALTERNATE RECORD KEY IS data-name-3 (WITH DUPLICATES) OR 'without'
FILE STATUS is data-name-4

Check Liant on-line manuals to expand on a definition of data-names 2 and 3

ISAM READ : The READ Statement (Relative and Indexed I/O)
---------------------------------------------------------

Format 1 :

READ file-name (NEXT) RECORD (WITH NO LOCK) (INTO identifier)
	AT END imperative-statement)

Format 2 :

READ file-name RECORD (WITH NO LOCK) (INTO identifier)
	KEY IS data-name
	INVALID KEY imperative-statement

Format 1 is used for files in sequential access mode.
Format 2 is used for files in random access mode or for files in dynamic 
access mode; I always specified 'Dynamic' for my ISAMs.

The KEY phrase may be specified only when the organization is 'ISAM'. 
When the KEY clause is present, data-name must be the name of one of the 
record keys associated with file-name. Data-name may be qualified.

Obviously more than above - but check out latest Liant manuals; they are 
up to Version 11 I believe.

-----------------------------------------------------------------------

Your informant had better take an RM Refresher Course. The extract I've 
given above is from Version 2.0b, obsolete and no longer supported and 
published in 1984 - so that makes it COBOL '78 ?

Jimmy
```

```
Refs:  RM/COBOL V6.5 for UNIX and Win95 Users Guide
          RM/COBOL V6.5 for UNIX and Win95 Language Reference Manual

Pete.

1)  There is no mention of 'ISAM' files in the above refs.
2)  Recognized file structures are 
     a) sequential 
     b) relative record
     c) indexed
3)  'Alternate-record keys' can be used in 'Read' and 'Start' statements.  Standard
     rules of the 'Read' and 'Start' statements apply.
4)  My Vista machine crapped out for the 7th time in 6 months the other day, and I 
     am now in the process of building an XP machine and will not install RMC until 
     I get back on XP.  I will be available for testing at that time.

sss

never known a pc to be more stable (one week of solid 24/7 disk
thrashing without a crash but that is another story). Lesson learned -
do not install an upgrade on to the operating system which requires
the new OS to 'guess' the parameters to run the applications by.
Instead, clean install the original OS and then install the upgrade
and patches. Then, finally, re-install your applications.

## Thread: COBOL in Commodore 64 — 4 msgs

### COBOL in Commodore 64

- **From:** x01001x <xemail@softhome.net>
- **Date:** 2008-07-02T20:03:25-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6f7b1399-b2cb-4cce-9efa-a3fcb9315299@59g2000hsb.googlegroups.com>`

```
How does one write and compile COBOL programs on a Commodore 64? I
thought the whole thing ran on BASIC code?
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL in Commodore 64

- **From:** "robertwessel2@yahoo.com" <robertwessel2@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2008-07-02T23:45:38-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8a09426f-13bf-4724-8321-4abb214a4649@8g2000hse.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<6f7b1399-b2cb-4cce-9efa-a3fcb9315299@59g2000hsb.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Jul 2, 10:03 pm, x01001x <xem...@softhome.net> wrote:
> How does one write and compile COBOL programs on a Commodore 64? I
> thought the whole thing ran on BASIC code?


With a text editor and a Cobol compiler, just like on any other
platform.

The C64 had a 6502 (actually a 6510), and while it certainly had a
BASIC interpreter in ROM, you could program it in any language that
you could get a compiler or interpreter for.  Pascal, C, assembler and
Logo were all available (as well as others).

Now whether or not anyone ever built a Cobol compiler for the C64 is
another question.  The C64 Wiki appears to list a product from Abacus,
but I don't know anything about it.
```

#### ↳ Re: Fujitsu or Microfocus

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com>
- **Date:** 2008-07-03T07:12:40-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cn3bk.12927$xZ.991@nlpi070.nbdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<6d3ij5Fl3lmU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
"Steve Rainbird" <steve.nospam.rainbird@mssint.nospam.com> wrote in message 
news:6d3ij5Fl3lmU1@mid.individual.net...
> One of my colleagues stated the other day
>
> "worldwide Fujitsu is more prevalent than MicroFocus"
>
> Is this true?

I don't know the answer, but whenever I see a claim like this I ask myself, 
"counting HOW?"

Does one application installed at ten sites  with 100 users each count as 
one, ten or one thousand?

Or does the count apply only to development systems?

How about multiple versions of the same application?

As my late sainted mother used to say, "figures don't lie, but liars 
figure."

MCM
```

#### ↳ Re: Fujitsu or Microfocus

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-07-04T00:13:46+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6d3u3pFnhl3U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<6d3ij5Fl3lmU1@mid.individual.net>`

```


"Steve Rainbird" <steve.nospam.rainbird@mssint.nospam.com> wrote in message 
news:6d3ij5Fl3lmU1@mid.individual.net...
> One of my colleagues stated the other day
>
> "worldwide Fujitsu is more prevalent than MicroFocus"
>
> Is this true?

Yes.

Fujitsu Corporation has more than 40 times the capitalization of MicroFocus. 
They also own ICL, a major UK computer provider, and Siemens, a major German 
computer provider..

Fujitsu Corporation is SO diverse and they report over a number of 
Geographical areas, but in IT alone (leaving aside devices like heat pumps 
etc. ) estimates place their capitalization in 2007 at around $US 45 
billion.

MicroFocus: 538 million UK pounds = (apprx) $US 1.028 billion.

>
> I am only talking about 'nix and Windows systems.

Neither company is forthcoming about their customer base in these areas.  As 
far as COBOL goes, judging by searches on the web and general discussions in 
forums, it looks like MF have the larger share of this market.

Fujitsu made a concerted effort to penetrate the workstation marketplace 
some years back (late 1990s), riding on the back of MicroFocus' failed VISOC 
product. (Fujitsu offered version 3 of their NetCOBOL and PowerCOBOL 
products free, along with tools to help migrate from MF). Although this was 
an agressive move by Fujitsu, in many ways they failed to press it home. 
They cut back on their support and laid off some very capable people, and 
the whole business of getting support (particularly outside the USA) became 
impossible.  As people dropped maintenance (they were paying for something 
that wasn't forthcoming), they lost the right to upgrade at an advantageous 
price, and so this gave them further incentive to move off Fujitsu. Fujitsu 
licensing required remote server registration, and transferring a licence 
from one machine to another was via a very unwieldy process and led to 
people simply giving up on it. Today, it is possible to use a virtual 
machine to circumvent it, which makes me wonder what all the fuss was about 
at the time. It isn't about trying to defraud Fujitsu; it is about having 
COBOL available 24/7. Apart from all this, COBOL is declining anyway, and it 
is simply more economic to define a migration strategy and get off COBOL for 
good. (For mainframe shops this tends to mean Java, SOA, and Web based 
applications. For others it means .NET, C#, SOA, component based development 
and Web based applications...)

MicroFocus has also had it's share of problems, but they have weathered them 
better than Fujitsu have.

Both Companies have excellent products (compilers and utilities... stock 
IDEs are still primitive compared to VS 2008 or the latest versions of 
Eclipse), but MF have targeted committed COBOL shops (mainly mainframe) 
where they can offer added value for COBOL development.

(Fujitsu have similar "mainframe compatibility" and services, but without 
maintenance and support it is much harder to get onto such sites. I know 
from my own experience there were a couple of occasions when I would have 
loved to recommend Fujitsu COBOL to a mainframe site and even set up and run 
pilot projects with it, but the risk was simply too high because I had 
already been burned with abysmal support, while doing a project on a 
non-mainframe site. I could not, in all conscience, recommend it.)

Fujitsu are a giant who could drop COBOL and never miss it; MicroFocus have 
their future much more closely tied to it. (Given the new wave at MF, and 
the astuteness of the new helmsman, I suspect this problem is already being 
addressed... :-))

Which horse should you back?

In my opinion, neither. Both good horses, but do you really want to gamble 
your future on COBOL, when there's no need to?

Pete.
```

You can't "call" an assembler attach macro.  You can, however, write
and IBM Assembler program that uses the ATTACH macro and call that
program.

<a id="t-e7fb3320ad"></a>
## Thread: contracting in New York city — 17 msgs

_Topics: [`Jobs, careers, recruiting, salary`](topics.md#jobs)_

### contracting in New York city

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-07-05T11:38:12+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6d7qisF1an1tU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
From time to time here in CLC we have discussed aspects of freelancing and 
some of the pitfalls to watch out for.

The whole contract programming scenario has undergone serious change during 
the last 30 years, at least, it has in Europe. I remember the heady days of 
cowboy agencies that were mostly unregulated,  with spreads of 70%, and 
cowboy contractors who would write CVs to fit the job and be quite happy to 
be fired after 3 months, when their incompetence became apparent, then go 
and repeat the process at the next unsuspecting client.

I know we've had discussions here about contracting and there have been some 
very good points raised.

However, New York city seems to be  still on the wild frontier. I was there 
on business once many years ago (on behalf of a recruitment agency back in 
the UK which I had an interest in) and wherever I went people kept making me 
outrageous deals in an effort to recruit me, even though I wasn't looking 
for a job... :-) I found the whole experience quite amusing. I encountered 
blatant racism, bigotry, and an insularity I have found nowhere else in the 
world. Most of the people I met had no idea about a world outside NY city 
and were not interested. Their attitude was that to live and work there was 
the epitome of any career and by being there you had actually "arrived". 
They couldn't understand why I would gently and politely decline a chance to 
live and work there. That was in the 1980s. I left with a feeling of 
affection for the diversity and sheer "in your face" brashness of the place 
:-). (And the food was amazing... always important in my book :-))

From posts made here regarding contracting and some of the practices used by 
recruitment agents, it seems to me like Americans have to put up with much 
more than their European counterparts. I'm not pretending for a moment that 
there are not dubious practices used by recruitment agencies in Europe, but 
in Germany, for example, interviews with the client are conducted with the 
agent present, so that all three parties can communicate openly (and 
politely), and in the UK agencies cannot get onto preferred supplier lists 
for major corporations unless they agree to limit their spread (usually to 
15%). They are "rewarded" by getting more placements and this keeps it 
viable for all parties.

I was interested to read this article which addresses recruitment in New 
York city for programmers. Although the writer is addressing .NET 
programmers, the advice given is sound for any kind of contracting. What 
interested me is why he would choose to single out New York city as 
requiring special attention... :-)

http://www.c-sharpcorner.com/UploadFile/Consulting/Article/mgold/NetogiationDotNet06022008005855AM/NetogiationDotNet.aspx

Comments?

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: contracting in New York city

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-07-04T22:31:36-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9dpt649gs7uocgbtdc6acq760maletv2l6@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<6d7qisF1an1tU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Sat, 5 Jul 2008 11:38:12 +1200, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

>I was interested to read this article which addresses recruitment in New 
>York city for programmers. Although the writer is addressing .NET 
>programmers, the advice given is sound for any kind of contracting. What 
>interested me is why he would choose to single out New York city as 
>requiring special attention... :-)

New York is a city run by amateurs. It's expensive because everything is so inefficient.
New Yorkers have the hubris to think their city is the commercial capital of the US, even
though they've never been anywhere else. Their bigotry is especially strong against The
South and New Jersey. In fact, the only industries based in New York are finance and
publishing. Finance (the stock market) is boom-and-bust and fraught with scandal.
Publishing is a sinking ship because it is stuck with obsolete technology.

New York seems like a foreign country where nothing works the same as the rest of the US. 
When I'm there, I keep saying "Back in the US they do it this way .. " 

Yes, the food is good, but it's better in Houston and Los Angeles. 

I know New York well because I was born and raised there.
```

highlighted in the article apply to agencies wherever in the world
that they operate.

One tactic that the article omitted is 'Making up the numbers' where
the agency has to put forward 6 people for a position but only has two
willing qualified candidates. They then look around for 4 bodies to
make up the numbers.

Something that the article hints at is that you as the prospective
employee are out to get the best possible deal for yourself, the
client is out to get the best possible deal for themselves and the
agent is out to make as much money as possible. It is of little
interest to the agency as to how good a fit you are for the position
if they can shoe-horn you in for long enough to get their pay-off.
Consequently, some agencies will give you the answers to aptitude
tests to allow you to get an in. As long as your ignorance does not
get discovered before the agents' pay cheques arrive so much the
better. Whether you will be happy in place or not does not enter the
mind of the agent. I think of this as 'body-shopping' or 'white slave
trading'.

I have only encountered two agents whom I would trust (out of the
hundreds I have spoken to). Neither is in the business any more.
```

Texas food outside Houston is generally mediocre. Houston food is influenced by a large

Chicago takes high end restaurants seriously, but everyday food is nothing special. I
judge cities by the quality of moderate priced restaurants and supermarkets. Houston
supermarkets are the best I've seen in the US. I never lived in New Orleans. If I had, I'd

I did a one year gig on Long Island (Suffolk county) in 2004. The insular attitude has not
changed. The biggest change I noticed was traffic congestion on two lane roads that used
to be country roads. And high property taxes, avg. $8-10K, that return little in services.
Snow removal is especially bad.

Towns on the water exclude non-residents (read: blacks from the City) from their beaches.

Northern NJ (Morris and Bergen counties) is similarly situated relative to NYC, but
attitude and services are much better. The countryside is beautiful. 

>Did you have any thoughts on the advice in the article, Robert? Would you 
>say this is accurate for NYC today?

It seemed applicable to contracting companies everywhere, not specifically NYC. New
Yorkers add an irritating accent and know-it-all attitude. 

Manhattan jobs used to pay higher than elsewhere. They no longer do. You are forced to
live in the suburbs and commute via train. I refuse to live that way. 

>I think most of us take the place where we are born and raised for granted, 
>and grow up believing there has to be something better somewhere else. 

I thought the same, and experience showed I was right. I lived in Los Angeles 10 years and
Texas 30 years. In the last 10 years, I lived in 15 US cities. All were better than NYC.
My favorites are Morristown NJ and the Northwest suburbs of Chicago, where I did 2 gigs.
The only one I hated was Long Island.

>Often, the passage of time and consequent mellowing allows us to see it

##### ↳ ↳ Re: contracting in New York city

- **From:** "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net>
- **Date:** 2008-07-05T11:19:04-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<t9Nbk.34$FY3.29@newsfe07.iad>`
- **References:** `<6d7qisF1an1tU1@mid.individual.net> <9dpt649gs7uocgbtdc6acq760maletv2l6@4ax.com>`

```

Robert <no@e.mail> wrote in message
news:9dpt649gs7uocgbtdc6acq760maletv2l6@4ax.com...
> On Sat, 5 Jul 2008 11:38:12 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

> Publishing is a sinking ship because it is stuck with obsolete technology.
>


What DO you mean by this?  Isn't publishing the very essence of the
internet?  Videos, blogs, web sites - all meant to get information or
opinions out there.

Or do you mean "publishing" in the much more restricted sense of words on
paper?  If so, you haven't got much of a case either.  You must assume that
the publishing industry has access to exactly the same technology as every
other industry.  You can correctly point out that newspaper readership is
declining.  You can also point out that book-publishing is experiencing
razor-thin profit margins.  But to say that the industry is sinking is
silly.  It's still adapting to the internet as is every other content-based
industry.  (An example of its adapting is the "publish/print on demand"
concept - print the book only when someone actually wants one, and soft or
no covers at that - an enormous boon for students if not the professors who
write the texts).  I'll remind you that nobody has yet made a portable
electronic book - no matter how many books you can download to it and no
matter how clear and large the screen and no matter how many pages you can
display at once - that has replaced even a minute percentage of the paper
book market.  Remember also that no less a person than Bill Gates has been
quoted as saying that if a report runs to more than 3 or 4 pages he'll print
it.  Newspaper readership has declined but hundreds of millions are still
printed and read each day.  I defy you to propose a technology that can
print and collate 350,000 copies af a 120-page newspaper (as in Winnipeg -
the Free Press) between 12:30a.m. and 3:30 a.m., on pulp paper, in a
standard newspaper-sized page, apart from the big rotary presses they use.
And these stopped using steam power long ago!

(Apropos of this - PD's book is to be publish-on-demand, n'est-ce pas?)

The telephone was supposed to replace the telegraph.  Well, it did, by about
1965, and it's only in the last ten years or so that you no longer have to
learn Morse to get your international ham license.

Radio was supposed to replace newspapers.
Films were supposed to replace live performances.
TV was supposed to replace theatres AND newspapers.
Now the internet is supposed to replace them all.

Well, let's wait and see.

PL
```

I also recall seeing posting from recruiters posing as 'normal people' 
who'd try to formulate strategies bu 'just asking' questions... good thing 
*those* days are gone!

>
>The whole contract programming scenario has undergone serious change during 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>and repeat the process at the next unsuspecting client.

My experience (limited to the US) is that the more things have changed, 
the more they have stayed the same; something about 'nothing new under the 
sun' was written a few millennia back.

>
>I know we've had discussions here about contracting and there have been some 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>world.

I found it matched only by the racism, bigorty and insularity I saw in 
London... oh, and Frankfurt... and then there was Tokyo... has anyone 
mentioned Paris?  New York, I'd say, is a financially egalitarian society 
and Having The Money is almost universally accepted; in other parts of the 
world a certain accent or set or manners accomplishes the same end.  

From what I've seen of the world 'folks is folks' and behave more-or-less 
similarly.

>Most of the people I met had no idea about a world outside NY city 
>and were not interested. Their attitude was that to live and work there was 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>:-). (And the food was amazing... always important in my book :-))

The food is still wonderful (I'm 'in town' for the three-day weekend for 
the Fourth of July and it is an eating-weekend... get food, doze, get up, 
walk the pug and chat with folks about how lovely she is, get more food, 
doze, lather, rinse, repeat).

[snip]

>I was interested to read this article which addresses recruitment in New 
>York city for programmers. Although the writer is addressing .NET 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>Comments?

What follows is, of course, my own limited experience and in no wise 
should be treated as a generalisation, either of contracting practises or 
the Human Condition:

Things are, more-or-less, the same all over.  Given the ease and 
efficiencies of modern communications is it rather easy to find out if 
what Plays in Pretoria will Work in Warsaw as well... in fact, it may well 
be a branch of the same firm which does the experimenting.  Selling what 
you don't have, lowballing an offer, saying that the final decision must 
be made by Oz, the Great and Terrible ('Nobody sees Oz... nobody's *ever* 
sees Oz!').

My usual response to such things nowadays (I was a bit more brash in the 
past) is a variant of 'Oh, what a shame... well, I bid you Good Hunting!'  
For example:

(P: is for Pimp, M: is for Me)

P: 'Gee, you're great... but what you're asking is out of the clent's 
range.'

M: 'Well, I hope they're happy with whoever they get at that rate, then, 
because it isn't going to be me.'

... or ...

P: 'Gee, you're great... but for something like this I'll have to speak 
with Mr McGleggenhacthler and I can tell you he isn't going to approve 
it'.

M: 'I've been presenting myself in business situations since... well, 
probably before your parents were born; I'd like to speak with Mr 
McGleggenhacthler myself and state my own case.  Have him give me a call 
when he's available, would you?

... or ...

P: 'I just can't make a profit at that rate!'

M: 'That's terrible, *everyone* has to make a profit somewhere... tell you 
what, let's all given-and-take on this one; call your contact at 
Accounting, have them give me a copy of your invoices and I'll take 75% of 
that number.'

As for the going-from-contract-to-employee scenario or the promise of a 
completion-bonus... I've addressed the former in other threads and the my 
response to the latter is 'If it is so certain then you won't mind sharing 
the risk... half up front, please, another quarter at midpoint and the 
balance on completion.'

DD
```

## Thread: END-PERFORM scope terminator — 17 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

### END-PERFORM scope terminator

- **From:** "Roger While" <simrw@sim-basis.de>
- **Date:** 2008-07-05T13:35:16+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<g4nm9l$pqu$01$1@news.t-online.com>`

```
Anybody know why the standard does not allow END-PERFORM
on an out-of-line PERFORM? (It is, of course, mandated for inline)

Roger
```

#### ↳ Re: END-PERFORM scope terminator

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com>
- **Date:** 2008-07-05T08:37:17-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<oOKbk.13113$xZ.5857@nlpi070.nbdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<g4nm9l$pqu$01$1@news.t-online.com>`

```
"Roger While" <simrw@sim-basis.de> wrote in message 
news:g4nm9l$pqu$01$1@news.t-online.com...
> Anybody know why the standard does not allow END-PERFORM
> on an out-of-line PERFORM? (It is, of course, mandated for inline)
???

Several standards allow these syntax constructions; a 'standard' by 
definition is simply a statement of "what should work."

However, your compiler (make, model and version not shown) apparently does 
not support the standard. (Which standard not shown).

Or maybe it does but you have not coded it correctly. (Code not shown).

MCM
```

#### ↳ Re: END-PERFORM scope terminator

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2008-07-05T13:38:02+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<g4ntfq$eso$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<g4nm9l$pqu$01$1@news.t-online.com>`

```
In article <g4nm9l$pqu$01$1@news.t-online.com>,
Roger While <simrw@sim-basis.de> wrote:
>Anybody know why the standard does not allow END-PERFORM
>on an out-of-line PERFORM? (It is, of course, mandated for inline)

I barely know why *I* allow things, let alone anyone else... but if I 
recall correctly and END-PERFORM terminates execution of statements which 
follow the preceding PERFORM while an 'undelimited' PERFORM directs 
execution to the statement following the specified paragraph or section 
named.

The delimiter of an 'original' PERFORM, then, seems to be the period (full 
stop) which terminates the structure (or range) specified by the 
imperative.

DD
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: END-PERFORM scope terminator

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com>
- **Date:** 2008-07-05T09:06:49-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5eLbk.13117$xZ.8657@nlpi070.nbdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<g4nm9l$pqu$01$1@news.t-online.com> <g4ntfq$eso$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:g4ntfq$eso$1@reader1.panix.com...
> The delimiter of an 'original' PERFORM, then, seems to be the period (full
> stop) which terminates the structure (or range) specified by the
> imperative.

Not quite DD, but close.

The delimeter of the original ('out of line' ) PERFORM would be the end of 
the performed paragraph or section, or until the single imperative statement 
'EXIT'  is executed... . unless the 'range' option you mention (PERFORM 
THRU) is used, in which case I generally get lost unless that "THRU" is 
simply a paragraph containing only the single imperative statement EXIT.

That is, a PERFORM'ed paragraph or section may contain one or more full 
stops which do NOT serve as a scope delimiter for the [out-of-line aka 
'original'] PERFORM statement.


MCM
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: END-PERFORM scope terminator

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2008-07-05T14:50:19+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<g4o1nb$ja5$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<g4nm9l$pqu$01$1@news.t-online.com> <g4ntfq$eso$1@reader1.panix.com> <5eLbk.13117$xZ.8657@nlpi070.nbdc.sbc.com>`

```
In article <5eLbk.13117$xZ.8657@nlpi070.nbdc.sbc.com>,
Michael Mattias <mmattias@talsystems.com> wrote:
>
><docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:g4ntfq$eso$1@reader1.panix.com...
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>Not quite DD, but close.

Story of my life, Mr Mattias.

>
>The delimeter of the original ('out of line' ) PERFORM would be the end of 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>simply a paragraph containing only the single imperative statement EXIT.

We may be dealing with variations between compilers, versions, platforms 
and garnishes, Mr Mattias... and the chance for me to code a few examples 
may have to wait until I get back to work.  I recall - and my memory is, 
admittedly, porous - that EXIT compiles to a the equivalent of an assembly 
NOP (No OPeration) and must be in its own paragraph and/or have its own 
period/full stop.

>
>That is, a PERFORM'ed paragraph or section may contain one or more full 
>stops which do NOT serve as a scope delimiter for the [out-of-line aka 
>'original'] PERFORM statement.

Hence my words '... which terminates the structure (or range)'.  For some 
moderately tedious examples:

PERFORM A87325-UPDATE-MASTER

... directs execution to the first imperative statement following the 
paragraph-name A87325-UPDATE-MASTER and (absent redirection by a GO TO or 
ALTER) returns execution to the next imperative following the PERFORM 
after execution encounters a paragraph-name following A87325-UPDATE-MASTER 
(unless A87325-UPDATE-MASTER is the last paragraph in the code in which 
case (etc)).

PERFORM name-1 THRU name-2 does more-or-less the same except the signal to 
return execution to the imperative following the PERFORM is the 
paragraph-name following name-2.

PERFORMing sections instead of paragraphs is, I believe, handled in a 
similar manner.

DD
k
```

#### ↳ Re: END-PERFORM scope terminator

- **From:** Robert Jones <rjones0@hotmail.com>

```
On 5 Jul, 12:35, "Roger While" <si...@sim-basis.de> wrote:
> Anybody know why the standard does not allow END-PERFORM
> on an out-of-line PERFORM? (It is, of course, mandated for inline)
>
> Roger

It is arguably superfluous.  The out of line PERFORM is terminated by
the next imperative or conditional statement, or by the period,
paragraph name, section name or end of program.  However, it might be
a conceptually helpful addition as is evidenced by some
implementations allowing it.

Robert
```

Mr Jones, I'm not quite sure how to read your statement.  Given the code:

A51000-EDIT-STUFF.

    IF VALID-COMBINATION
        CONTINUE
    ELSE
       PERFORM 9999-ALLES-GEHT-KERFLOOIE  THRU 9999-AGF-EX.

        PERFORM (etc).
...

A51286-EDIT-SOME-MORE.

    ELSE
        PERFORM A51300-NOM-DU-CHIEN  THRU  A51300-EX.

...

A51286-EX.
    EXIT.

Now... where do you see the statement PERFORM A51286-EDIT-SOME-MORE  THRU  
A51286-EX as 'being terminated'?  

(I see it as transferring program execution to the first statement (in 
this case the IF) following the paragraph-name A51286-EDIT-SOME-MORE, 
nothing more, nothing less.)

DD

#### ↳ Re: END-PERFORM scope terminator

- **From:** Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-07-07T01:20:29-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4078721a-f049-4d40-ace0-17742e9d2520@m45g2000hsb.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<g4nm9l$pqu$01$1@news.t-online.com>`

```
On Jul 5, 11:35 pm, "Roger While" <si...@sim-basis.de> wrote:
> Anybody know why the standard does not allow END-PERFORM
> on an out-of-line PERFORM? (It is, of course, mandated for inline)
>
> Roger

Conditional statements require their scope to be terminated, either by
a full stop or by an appropriate scope terminator.

Imperative statements are self contained and do not have scope
terminators.

Out of line PERFORMs are imperative and do not have scope.

In line PERFORMs are conditional in that they do have scope and thus
need terminating.
```

#### ↳ Re: END-PERFORM scope terminator

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-07-09T01:17:08+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<okUck.192491$fz6.35831@fe08.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<g4nm9l$pqu$01$1@news.t-online.com>`

```
"Roger While" <simrw@sim-basis.de> wrote in message 
news:g4nm9l$pqu$01$1@news.t-online.com...
> Anybody know why the standard does not allow END-PERFORM
> on an out-of-line PERFORM? (It is, of course, mandated for inline)
>
> Roger
>
I have been away and missed some of this thread.  However, I think that Richard 
has given the correct answer.

The '85 Standard introduced both "scope terminators" and "in-line performs".

With the exception of END-PERFORM, all other scope terminators do one of two 
things:

1) turn a conditional statement into an imperative statement, e.g.
    Read xyz
       At End
          Display  "This is conditional"

versus
    Read xyz
       At End
          Display  "This is imperative"
    End-Read

(a period will also terminate a conditional statement, but it will NOT make it 
imperative)

2) "documentary only" e.g.
      Read XYZ
      End-Read

****

As far as END-PERFORM goes, it indicates the "termination" of a block of code 
which begins with a PERFORM (without a procedure name) and makes that "block of 
code" available for use where any imperative statement may be used.

Therefore, END-PERFORM makes no "required" sense for an out-of-line PERFORM 
statement which is (by definition) always an imperative statement.  It could be 
allowed as documentary (and I think some implementations may do this as an 
extension).  However, it would never serve any syntactic sense for an 
out-of-line perform.  It may also be noted that at least one implementation 
(Micro Focus) allows it to be omitted for in-line PERFORMs (especially when 
followed by a period/full-stop).

  ***

P.S.  For those who have replied indicating that the "scope" of an out-of-line 
perform is somewhere at the end of the PERFORMed code, this is NOT how the COBOL 
Standard (ANSI, ISO, or any other that I know of) uses the term.  "Scope" is 
used as a lexical term and not (usually?) in the sense of "under the control 
of".
```

#### ↳ Re: END-PERFORM scope terminator

It doesn't add anything, as this kind of perform can't have multiple

help.   So why force compiler makers to add it?

phrases any place where it can't also be MEANINGFUL (i.e. to terminate a

## Thread: END-PERFORM scope terminator — 3 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

### Re: END-PERFORM scope terminator

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com>
- **Date:** 2008-07-05T09:15:06-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<TlLbk.13120$xZ.10490@nlpi070.nbdc.sbc.com>`

```
>> The delimiter of an 'original' PERFORM, then, seems to be the period 
>> (full
>> stop) which terminates the structure (or range) specified by the
>> imperative.

I may have misread this. I read it as....

"The delimiter of an 'original' PERFORM, then, seems to be the period (full 
stop),  which terminates the structure (or range) specified by the 
imperative."

...instead of


"The delimiter of an 'original' PERFORM, then, seems to be the period (full 
stop) which terminates the structure (or range) specified by the 
imperative."

.. as written.

The difference is the comma I mentally inserted after "(full stop)" which 
changes the entire meaning from "any full stop"  to "the final full stop 
found in the named paragraph, section or range."

EXIT would not longer figure in the second case, as an EXIT for out-of-line 
perform must be the entire paragraph of the named "THRU" range.

Damn, one little comma changes a lot, huh?

MCM
```

<a id="t-d852e44c15"></a>
## Thread: EVALUATE — 18 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

### EVALUATE

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-07-07T22:04:25+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6de81bF25b9nU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
I know we've discussed EVALUATE here on a number of occasions, but I'm 
wondering if anyone can shed light on the following:

     evaluate MOST-SQLSTATE
        when '00000' *> OK
             move zero to MOST-file-status
        when '02000' *> EOF or record not found
             if MOST-GetRandom
                move '23' to MOST-file-status
             else
                move '10' to MOST-file-status
        when '23000' *> Duplicate primary key on insert
             move '22' to MOST-file-status
        when other *> Catch-all... error and meaning are in
                          *> the interface SQLSTATE and SQLMsg
             move '90' to MOST-file-status
     end-evaluate
     .

The second 'when' can occur when random access is being done, in which case 
it means the required record (row) was not found, or it can occur when 
sequential access via a cursor is being done, in which case it indicates 
EOF.

I was kind of surprised when the Fujitsu compiler threw this code out saying 
that I couldn't use a conditional statement (IF) here as an imperative one 
was required.

Fair enough. I changed it to read as follows:

     evaluate MOST-SQLSTATE
        when '00000' *> OK
             move zero to MOST-file-status
        when '02000' *> EOF or record not found
             evaluate TRUE
                  when MOST-Getrandom
                           move '23' to MOST-file-status
                  when other
                           move '10' to MOST-file-status
             end-evaluate
        when '23000' *> Duplicate primary key on insert
             move '22' to MOST-file-status
        when other *> Catch-all... error and meaning are in
                   *> the interface SQLSTATE and SQLMsg
             move '90' to MOST-file-status
     end-evaluate
     .

This was accepted OK and the code executes as expected.

Now, please correct me if I'm wrong, but isn't EVALUATE a conditional 
statement? (It requires a terminator or scope delimiter).

It seems inconsistent to me that the original code was not accepted, but a 
different conditional was. I don't know whether this is the Fujitsu 
implementation, or whether it is the COBOL standard, but I would've thought 
that at the time the standard syntax for this was being considered
very little would be gained by NOT allowing conditions to be checked within 
the "arms" of an EVALUATE.  I tend to use EVALUATE as a CASE statement and 
so I keep them very simple (never use ALSO) and find them very useful for 
separating mutually exclusive processes at a fairly high level. I'm 
surprised they don't accommodate IF statements, and I can't see why they 
wouldn't.

Any comments?

Pete.
```

The diagnostic message is misleading.

On 'reasons' versus 'excuses': decades on back a German project director 
interrupted my explanation of a technical matter with 'I don't want to 
hear excuses, I want to be told things are working!'... and I recalled 
reading Wittgenstein asking about the difference between a symptom and a 
criterion so I replied 'You are not being given excuses, you are being 
given reasons... perhaps the difference between the two is that a 'reason' 
is what a subordinate says and an 'excuse' is what a superior hears.'

On the mixing of IF and EVALUATE: whenever possible I use this technique, 
first one, then the other; I've yet to run across anyone, either reviewing 
or maintaining the code, who has said it complicates matters.

On the error codes being used... I do not know how MOST-SQLSTATE relates 
to the SQLCODES of my youth but as I recall it the notfound/end condition 
was +100, not 200.

DD

>>> On 7/7/2008 at 6:45 AM, in message <g4t35a$lde$1@reader1.panix.com>,
<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote:
> On the error codes being used... I do not know how MOST-SQLSTATE relates 
> to the SQLCODES of my youth but as I recall it the notfound/end 
> condition 
> was +100, not 200.

He is checking for SQLSTATE '02000', not SQLCODE +100.

Both mean the same thing.

db2 => ? sql100


SQL0100W  No row was found for FETCH, UPDATE or DELETE; or the result of
      a query is an empty table.

Explanation:

One of the following conditions is true:
*  No row was found that meets the search conditions specified in an
   UPDATE or DELETE statement.
*  The result of a SELECT statement was an empty table.
*  A FETCH statement was executed when the cursor was positioned after
   the last row of the result table.
*  The result of the SELECT used in an INSERT statement is empty.

No data was retrieved, updated, or deleted.

User response:

No action is required. Processing can continue.

 sqlcode: +100

 sqlstate: 02000
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EVALUATE

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-07-08T12:23:16+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6dfqbmF2dlgoU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<6de81bF25b9nU1@mid.individual.net> <6deb9uF25i32U1@mid.individual.net> <g4t35a$lde$1@reader1.panix.com> <4871FF1F.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <g4tlk1$9ll$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```


<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:g4tlk1$9ll$1@reader1.panix.com...
> In article <4871FF1F.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>,
<snip>
>
>> sqlcode: +100
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> superior?

I can only state why *I* use SQLSTATE, rather than SQLCODE (although, 
depending on the shop standards, I have used both)

SQLSTATE is governed by an ANSI standard which makes it consistent (for the 
most part) across RDBMS; SQLCODE is subject to more vendor interpretation. 
(I have six different RDBMS on my system and use 3 of them regularly. My 
preference is for SQL Server  (along with about 300 million other users...) 
but I recognise that there are NO BAD RDBMS these days.  For my own stuff I 
use MS ACCESS and or SQL Server, but I like to think that the code is 
transportable to other RDBMS. Using SQLSTATE helps to ensure that.)

These days I am getting more and more into LINQ and functional programming 
where it really doesn't matter too much because the return is handled by 
standard exception programming in the Language (C# in my case). In the 
sample posted, I am using ESQL because it is for a client who is 
transitioning to RDB from ISAM, in COBOL. I believe ESQL is already obsolete 
and certainly much less useful than LINQ, but the reality is that, like many 
obsolete things, there will be life in it for some time yet... :-)

SQLSTATE uses a structured coding where the first two digits represent a 
class of error, and the next three give details of the actual error. (All 
five digits can be in hex, so this allows vendors to implement and document 
their own range of platform specific errors, without changing the agreed 
standard classes.) Obviously, a three digit code can't do that as easily.

If you are writing generalised or system data access components (which I am 
in this case), as opposed to applications, which really are only interested 
in the broad classes of error, you may need to get more details on exactly 
what went wrong, if something goes wrong.

In my opinion, SQLSTATE serves this purpose better than SQLCODE.

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: EVALUATE

- **From:** Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-07-07T12:11:13-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<244e3cf5-89d8-4cae-a579-338b73dc6196@c58g2000hsc.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<6de81bF25b9nU1@mid.individual.net> <6ts374prmvs4aiuatssc450vkdt864tovj@4ax.com>`

```
On Jul 7, 10:48 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
> On Mon, 7 Jul 2008 22:04:25 +1200, "Pete Dashwood" <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
> wrote:
…[34 more quoted lines elided]…
> The diagnostic message is misleading.

It is not misleading at all. All that is required is to understand the
difference between a conditional statement and an imperative one.

'IF' is a conditional statement. 'IF something END-IF' is an
imperative statement.  That is, any conditional statement that is
correctly scope terminated is imperative.

Some compilers don't care about this, other follow the standard.
```

delimiter. So, knowledge of the difference between a conditional and an 
imperative statement is not ALL that is required; some reasonable care and 
attentention must also be exercised :-)

Pete.
```

<a id="t-53510b0ce8"></a>
## Thread: How competent are we? — 62 msgs

### How competent are we?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-07-07T22:53:58+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6deauaF224l5U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
It is interesting to me to see the differences between the world of COBOL 
and the world outside that, and try to understand both.

COBOL evolved in an era when there was no computer science (or only very 
vestigial traces of it), when everything was powered from a central 
processor that simply interacted through dumb peripherals, and that mode of 
operation was perfectly adequate for "data processing".

Most of us are coming to accept that the world has moved on, and computer 
programming today has to address much more complex environments than the 
simple COBOL model ever did.

Here is a competency matrix which shows what most of the young programmers 
of today are expected to be familiar with:

http://www.indiangeek.net/wp-content/uploads/Programmer%20competency%20matrix.htm

I found it quite sobering and I was far from level 3 in more than one 
area... (I'm taking immediate steps to improve my competency... :-))

I think it gives insight into the way that things are addressed now and what 
is important. Notice the emphasis on tool and IDE knowledge, the 
understanding required of data and data structures, and knowledge of reuse, 
components, and OO taken as read.

I really liked the level 1 database criterion: "Thinks EXCEL is a 
database".... :-)

Pete.
```

[snip]

>I really liked the level 1 database criterion: "Thinks EXCEL is a 
>database".... :-)

What gave me a smile was 'With the increase in outsourcing of programming 
tasks to places where English is not the native tongue this issue has 
become more prominent. I know of several projects that failed because the 
programmers could not understand what the intent of the communication 
was.'

Has anyone ever seen something similar happen with in-house development?  
Then there was 'Code assumptions are verified using asserts, code flows 
naturally - no deep nesting of conditionals or methods'... and that can be 
rather tricky to achieve, expecially when the code is about decisions made 
in systems (manufacturing, insurance, finance, regulatory compliance) 
which are, by their very nature, deeply nested and anything *but* 
'natural'.

DD

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How competent are we?

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "klshafer@att.net" <klshafer@att.net>
- **Date:** 2008-07-17T15:04:59-07:00

- **Message-ID:** `<3c3e2d40-02ee-45b4-b387-e3d286662d59@56g2000hsm.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<6deauaF224l5U1@mid.individual.net> <468097a6-fd06-40e0-87c9-91b4b4088aae@l42g2000hsc.googlegroups.com> <6dfp4tF28hv6U1@mid.individual.net> <g6tm7452dp3olsdod6768q8he08lqk362d@4ax.com> <6e23thF50o0tU1@mid.individual.net> <90168972-2d23-4e3c-b424-b8625801112f@e39g2000hsf.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Jul 14, 10:02 pm, Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>As for "Maintains a blog in which personal insights and thoughts on
>programming are shared". Yeah, I bet he does, he listens with his
>mouth.

> > Pete D:  It isn't about bragging rights.
>

> Excuse me, but the whole point of a blog is 'bragging'.

Richard's observations, though stinging, seem to me to be right on
target.

The "uncharitable" part of me (yes, the part I am trying to subdue, in
deference to the more "charitable" part of me), would rejoin with the
following...

1. Notice the duplicated entire _row_ entries under "languages with
professional experience" and "languages exposed to." Would you trust a
professional who is unable to "normalize" a spreadsheet to normalize
your database model?

2. I printed it out from the IE 6 browser, in Landscape. Yep, it
truncated off the last column. Won't even print on a printer. Yeah,
I've heard it before from the young'uns, there's always _reason_ why
it won't print. It's screen resolution, or it's the browser, or it's
this thing or that thing, and OK, I know they have a reason, but all
of the reasons sound like bullsh*t to me.  I know that _some_ web
pages can print on an 8 x 11 1/2 piece of paper because some _do_.
Maybe even half, or _most_. So why can't the rest of them do it?
What good is his high-falutin' "levels" if he can't execute a simple
Print?

3. Confuses product specifics with abstract concepts, as in his
references to VSS and CVS. So if you're using another version control
product you're not Level 1 or 2 or 3? And doesn't include c-shell or
korn-shell in his scripting languages.

4. And, -finally-, agreeing with Richard, raises blogging (gasp!) as
the 3rd-nth-degree of professionalism, and places it under the
"Knowledge" :-) category.

Now from the more "charitable" side of Ken :-)...

It's just that I'm _uncomfortable_ with this "competency matrix", for
some reasons I can identify, and for others I cannot (no matter, CLC
will help me figure it out.) Certainly raising "blogging" to such
stellar heights bothers me. If I may paraphrase the 'Doc (do I have
your permission ;-) ), it does strike me as "Look Ma! I'm a Blogger!"

The presence of that alone probably does not rise to the occasion of
offense. It is what is _missing_. No mention of ACM. Of IEEE. Of any
participation in _any_ professional "society", and contributing
therein. And I think that is some of what Richard illuminated for us.
The "new way" to establish "culture" is to Blog, and have "links" to
all the other Bloggers that you agree with, or who you admire. (Yes,
there are similarities to what we do ourselves on CLC, but
differences, which I don't want to take the time to get into right
now.)

This is part of a broader "movement" that is altogether unsettling to
me: the substituion of "virtual" communities for "real" communities.
Yes the web is a wonderful, democratizing thing. And yet, I am left,
well, a little "cold" when I reflect on that. The same thing is
happening in the erosion of membership in Elks, in Rotary, in the
Masonic Lodges, in the VFW and the American Legion halls. Hey, the
young people just don't have the time, can't pry ourselves away from
the notebook/Wi-Fi and web. Time was, "paying your dues" and "rites of
passage" used to count for more. (I think those concepts will still
apply in the future, but increasingly as "esoterica", in a mysical, or
even, heretical, "undergound".)

For a well-considered, and I think related, treatise on this, take a
look at _Voyage in the Agile Memeplex_, by Philippe Kruchten,
published in ACM _Queue_:

http://www.acmqueue.org/modules.php?name=Content&pa=showpage&pid=501&page=1

You will not be disappointed.

Ken
```

 >> processing".

 R> It is arguable whether what is called 'computer science' is in fact
 R> science at all.

 R> I am sure that there was more actual 'science' being applied in
 R> software in the 1950s because the programmers were more involved with
 R> the designing and building of the machines.

 R> Of course computing and computers developed from business machines
 R> that went back to Pascal (Blaise not Wirth) so Cobol implemented what

despite entering it via the front panels switches).

to compile a Cobol program of 2000-3000 lines on a 2 or 4 tape based machine.

On Jul 8, 8:16 pm, "Vince Coen" <VBCoenDesp...@btconnect.com> wrote:
> Hello Richard!
>
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
> to it.

While RPG may have been _one_ of the languages used, it was certainly
not the only one. COBOL comes from 1959 or so and was a mixture of
other, prior, languages that were also developed to process tasks that
were previously the realm of unit record equipment.

Actually, RPG was used on hybrid machines, such as the Univac/ICL 1004
where a plugboard 'compiler' processed the program card pack into its
1000 word memory where this ran. The savings were enormous: one
plugboard plus a few dozen card packs could replace all the previous
plugboard programs.

But RPG was not implemented on ICL 1900 computers until RPG2 on the
2903. The 1900 had NICOL, RAPIDWRITE, Compact COBOL and COBOL (plus
several others).

> I started using Cobol in 1963 on a 2 different M/F's a ICL 1501 and a
> IBM 1401 (but this one was easier to program in machine code, it was quicker
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Vince

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How competent are we?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-07-15T11:07:51+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6e24i7F488ajU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<6deauaF224l5U1@mid.individual.net> <f6d574hsb81m8ahptgfsjv6j9544mgfpjt@4ax.com> <5btm74ht69oqn8k03tj2f54cf38qgs2knk@4ax.com>`

```


"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:5btm74ht69oqn8k03tj2f54cf38qgs2knk@4ax.com...
> On Mon, 07 Jul 2008 21:31:46 -0300, Clark F Morris
> <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> customers want.

The "new thing" is to approach testing in a different way from the 
traditional one. This is because the emphasis is no longer on source code 
and the maintenance of it. We've discussed here many times the need for 
regression testing in an environment where encapsulated components are being 
implemented. There is no such need, but people used to an environment where 
one small change to source code can have disastrous effects on downstream 
code simply don't get it.

Testing is still required to ensure that customers get what they want, but 
the difference is that the customers are involved right throughout the 
process and have been party to the development of each component and seen it 
working based on use cases. They have been instrumental in getting it 
changed as it developed, in line with their priorities. This has a big 
impact on the need for formal testing as we traditionally understood it.

Testing in this environment can be generated from the source code. This 
takes care of the purely technical fact that all pathways are executed and 
checked. Getting code to do what users want is taken care of by the joint 
application development with users who write the use cases.

Having programmers test, then users test, then regression testing everything 
when some part of it changes is simply unnnecessary and definitely not the 
ONLY way to ensure that working code both functions correctly and meets user 
requirements.

Pete.
```

But I have to agree with Richard, Erlang, Oz and Prolog are nice if you

concurrent programming, you shouldn't be doing concurrent programming.  
It is easy enough to get tripped up when you know all the balls you are 
keeping in the air, but to have them hidden from you will really invite 
an award of feline hindquarters.

But it was quite cute.

#### ↳ Re: How competent are we?

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2008-07-14T09:42:21-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<u9sm74hoq3hm519v3e1sm90qqk6v9n5e15@4ax.com>`

```
On Mon, 7 Jul 2008 22:53:58 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>http://www.indiangeek.net/wp-content/uploads/Programmer%20competency%20matrix.htm
>
>I found it quite sobering and I was far from level 3 in more than one 
>area... (I'm taking immediate steps to improve my competency... :-))

But that test is still with regards to tools.   One can be a very
competent programmer without understanding, say linked-lists (that
being the first basic skill mentioned).    If the tool (in this case a
database), handles data retrieval in the background, why should we
need to know anything more than what is needed to make a choice
between options that the database gives us?

I remember learning a bunch of sort algorithms - then for my whole
career we used the sort utility that came with my environment.
Actually I have coded some table sorts - sometimes comb sorts, but if
my compiler had a table-sort option, I never would have done so.

Other options are specialized as well - for instance, knowing one's
way around one IDE doesn't mean knowing one's way around a different
IDE.

##### ↳ ↳ Re: How competent are we?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-07-15T11:52:00+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6e274vF4vqt8U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<6deauaF224l5U1@mid.individual.net> <u9sm74hoq3hm519v3e1sm90qqk6v9n5e15@4ax.com>`

```


"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:u9sm74hoq3hm519v3e1sm90qqk6v9n5e15@4ax.com...
> On Mon, 7 Jul 2008 22:53:58 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> being the first basic skill mentioned).

I believe that is arguable. It depends on how you define "competent 
programmer".

If there are fundamental techniques that should be part of a programmer's 
tool kit and they are not, then you might well argue that, no matter how 
good the person is in other areas, there is still a competency that is 
missing.

Consider a more pertinent example in COBOL land...

Would you consider a programmer to be "very competent" if they couldn't 
describe and write a "three way split" to update a master file? No? Neither 
would I... In fact, in my book, they would need to demonstrate understanding 
of the process to merge and update ANY number of files before I'd grant them 
"VERY competent..." (and to get "expert" they'd have to be capable of 
explaining it to others, in a way where others could also grasp it...)


>If the tool (in this case a
> database), handles data retrieval in the background, why should we
> need to know anything more than what is needed to make a choice
> between options that the database gives us?

You are describing the difference between a driver and a mechanic.

Do mechanics make better drivers? They are certainly gentler on the 
machinery and get less frustrated with it.

Most of the top class competitive drivers (both formula and rally) have a 
knowledge of how their cars work. (It might not be detailed enough to allow 
them to replace a gudgeon pin or crankshaft, but you can bet they know what 
function is served by these components...) That knowledge can make the 
difference between nursing a failing component and finishing the race, or 
leaving a trail of hardware on the track and getting pushed to the pits.

Certainly, the environments we work in make differing demands on our skill 
and knowledge, and it may be possible to "get away with" not knowing about 
linked lists in an environment where you are never likely to use them, or 
get away with not understanding a basic three way split if you are more 
concerned with process control of flow through an oil refinery. But 
environments change. If you call yourself a computer programmer you should 
have a knowledge of computer programming. The reason I posted the matrix was 
because it demonstrates how many of the core skills required today are not 
the core skills that we had to acquire as COBOL programmers. The real world 
is an OO one, databases are not just repositories that get manipulated from 
code but are "smart" subsytems with their own stored procedures, and 
computer science provides solutions and tools that are not "dreamed of in 
your philosophy". (Things like regular expressions, delegation, and 
reflection are facilities not available  in COBOL yet I find I can't do 
without them in everyday programming, once I grasped what they do... I am 
still staggered by the power of these things...)


>
> I remember learning a bunch of sort algorithms - then for my whole
> career we used the sort utility that came with my environment.

I started my career (1965) by writing a sort program for an NCR 500... :-) 
There were no sort utilities... and few recognised algorithms. I went to the 
library (remember them...? they were places where you got information before 
the Internet was invented...:-)) and found what I could about sorting (very 
little, but I learned what a Fibonacci series was...). Today people are 
given a number of sort algorithms just as you were, and they end up using 
the utility available with their platform, just as you did. But are they any 
worse off for recognising there are other possible solutions?

I don't think so.

> Actually I have coded some table sorts - sometimes comb sorts, but if
> my compiler had a table-sort option, I never would have done so.
>
See comments re driver/mechanic above.

> Other options are specialized as well - for instance, knowing one's
> way around one IDE doesn't mean knowing one's way around a different
> IDE.

It does mean the learning curve will be shorter and less steep when you get 
to the next IDE. Shortly after teaching myself the fundamentals of Visual 
Studio 2005 (from downloadable videos and lurking in a few forums) I was 
working in a place where they used Eclipse. It would have been meaningless 
to me if I had only had my knowledge of SPF as an IDE.  Within 10 minutes I 
was able to productively use Eclipse (I prefer VS, but there is no point in 
getting emotional about software...:-))

The point is that the more knowledge you have, the easier it is to acquire 
new knowledge. The "yearning for learning" feeds on itself.

Coming back to the thread topic to finish this mail, our competence is 
judged solely by how successful we are at getting the job done. I agree with 
you that sometimes this doesn't require in depth knowledge. But I contend 
that having more in depth knowledge is desirable, and very often useful in 
ways you don't imagine at the time you acquire the knowledge.

Pete.
```

Richard wrote:
> On Jul 15, 11:52 am, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> deal with this.

And there, in a nutshell, is the problem.

The driver no longer knows, and is incapable of analysing the problem. 
Asking that he becomes competent in his discipline is dismissed as an 
archaic view.

The parallels with the modern "computing expert" who palms off the analysis 
of a problem to the real gurus in the back room whilst waffling in support 
of a PhD thesis - making up pompous skills-analysis matrices for instance - 
are obvious. These people simply don't WANT to know how to analyse a 
problem - delegate to the less-glamorous underlings and wait for the result.

Which of course prevents the gurus from being gurus as the enquirer has no 
intention of being taught.

Perhaps a more relevant question we should be asking is "How competent is 
the boss?" And the usual answer is "Well, he read a book once." As for the 
academics - well, there's those who do and then there's those who teach. Or 
preach.

>> On Jul 15, 11:52 am, "Pete Dashwood"

Fair comment. I have encountered people who do fit this description. But I 
have also encountered many who don't.

The point of my post was that the the IT industry in 2008 is much broader 
than it was in 1968, but the required levels of entry expertise are also 
higher. In the old days we could teach someone from the business COBOL in a 
couple of weeks, and let them loose. We can't do that any more. It is partly 
because we can't do that any more that COBOL as a language is declining.

Pete.

I keep seeing you make this last statement.  And you are not the only one.
We have Professor here who is always spouting the same mantra.  Enough so
that the last course we offered using COBOL has finally changed to Java.
Too bad my contacts in industry don't seem to agree.  Everytime I mention
to one of the local COBOL shop managers that what I hear they roll on the
floor laughing.  They continue to maintain millions of lines of COBOL with
no plans to migrate any of it to another language.  I have been back to
visit the place where I last worked writing COBOL.  That was over 20 years
ago.  They are still maintaining programs that have my name in the comments.

I have looked for jobs as a COBOL programmer again.  I would love to end my
career doing COBOL again.  There are lots of jobs most int he six figures
catagory.  The only thing that keeps me from taking one is not the language
but the OS.  Most of them require current CICS experience which I don't have,
having moved from IBM to Unisys a long, long time ago.

So, tell us the truth.  What is the real reason you have such a large axe
to grind against COBOL?

bill

And there's another "competence" example.

We have managers who haven't a clue, "HR" sections (whoever invented that 
term deserves retrospective abortion) who have less, and "recruiters" who 
have an eye only for their commissions.

These last two groups filter out candidates who don't have the "essential" 
skill "CICS/COBOL/DB2" (of which "COBOL" is simply an abbreviation.) In 
fact, to state the obvious, most CICS/COBOL coding can be completed by 
simple cut-paste and tinker - if some facility like MANTIS doesn't remove 
the need for CICS knowledge almost completely. DB2 is just another database, 
despite the hype from the zealots.

But what has more influence is the yap-yap from the PhD students. Like the 
last job I was in - the PhD student, having never encountered COBOL before 
recommended changing a fundamental file-layout copybook, then 
blanket-recompiling 650 programs. Since I was such a fusspot, she'd make the 
supreme effort and test the resultant executables - she'd even sacrifice a 
whole day doing that. If I didn't like it, she'd just spend a week learning 
COBOL and do it herself. Well, she's still working there - I'm not.

```
On Wed, 16 Jul 2008 12:18:16 +0800, "billious" <billious_1954@hotmail.com> wrote:

>Bill Gunshannon wrote:
>> In article <6e3ei0F51mefU1@mid.individual.net>,
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
>COBOL and do it herself. Well, she's still working there - I'm not.

Changes in file layout are easy if IO is isolated in called programs. Old programs,
without recompiling, get the old v1.0 layout; new programs that ask for it get the v2.0
layout. Isolating the Data Layer is common practice in client server. I used to do it on
ALL my mainframe sysstems. I converted one mission critical system from VSAM to
database-like VSAM just by rewriting data layers. Old programs thought the file structures
hadn't changed.
```

```
Robert wrote:
> On Wed, 16 Jul 2008 12:18:16 +0800, "billious"
> <billious_1954@hotmail.com> wrote:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> thought the file structures hadn't changed.

Oooh - that's a big IF. Should be in bold red 72pt caps.

The system in question was written in ACU COBOL on a micro system, derived 
from a WANG system derived from a 1970s IBM system. It had been designed by 
an accountant and maintained by "graduates" of the new school.

Their priority was firmly in the "make it look good" department - so the 
users had to choose the printer for EVERY printout EVERY time. There had 
been no attempt at data-analysis so the SINGLE SKU record contained such 
things as purchase-order history, sales-history and current stock figures 
FOR EACH BRANCH in an array. As the number of branches had increased, the 
problems with record-locking had also increased (for some unknown reason.) 
Their solution was to simply ignore I/O errors in the programs that were 
most commonly-used (matrix-level 0?) There had been frantic attempts to 
implement proper record-locking procedures - pity the programmers had no 
idea of what they were doing - there were READ NO-LOCKs even on read-only 
files; it was just another example of "apply the curing mantra and all will 
be well" without actually UNDERSTANDING what the locking business was all 
about. Ask the guru, and his solution will be applicable in all 
circumstances.

There were also traces of earlier expansions - the occasional limit of 8 
branches scanned in the loop rather than the current 15 branches. No idea of 
setting a symbolic constant if you had to follow the accountant's path to 
damnation - just "15" repeated possibly hundreds of times in each program. 
The object was of course to implement more branches. Every new branch needed 
a new individual despatch-docket program - and each of the existing 
despatch-docket programs had to be adjusted to recognise the new branch. 
None of this new-fangled idea of finding branch details in a file like for 
instance address-details or the array-index for the branch - oh no, each 
reference was made to an individually-named field; SMITHTOWN-INVENTORY, not 
INVENTORY(BRANCH-INDEX).

Management's understanding may be exemplified by their monthly sales-figures 
report, lines of branch, columns of sales-category. The assistant general 
manager claimed that the grand total balanced with the actual banking. All 
of the columns added up correctly, all of the rows added up correctly. Pity 
that the major two sales categories for the headquarters "branch" showed 
zero sales, but they should have been about 40% of the total - not an 
insignificant hole. He claimed that these figures were "suppressed." Er - in 
that case, the total wouldn't balance with the banking - and I'd just 
changed the program to add a new branch - no sign of suppressing the 
figures, they could only have been misallocated. But that's the way the 
report had been for years - so therefore it had to be correct.

So - good theory about isolating Data Layers. Studying for a PhD, are you? 
;)
```
