##### ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-07-19T02:12:56+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6ebmn9F6b9lrU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <OXZfk.17988$Ri.5145@flpi146.ffdc.sbc.com>`

```


"Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com> wrote in message 
news:OXZfk.17988$Ri.5145@flpi146.ffdc.sbc.com...
> "x01001x" <xemail@softhome.net> wrote in message 
> news:b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com...
<snipped good responses I have no issue with>

> Of course, well-written software should have a long useful life; which 
> should tell you something vis-a-vis my opinion on today's quality versus 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I don't think the quality of today's programming is any worse than it was in 
the last century, Michael.

Today, it's a different environment. Many of the things we "took as read" 
and had drilled into us are not relevant in an OO component based approach. 
If a component has been tested and is known to work, why you would you 
retest it every time it is reused? You are more interested in testing to 
make sure it plugs into the new application OK (its interfaces...) rather 
than testing every method it contains again, for example. Because things are 
not necessarily done the way they were does not, of itself, mean the quality 
is inferior.

Whether you use iteration and interaction to evolve a program, or use a 
formal specification and the stages of the Waterfall to ensure it's quality, 
it still has to perform in production. The criticism that we didn't consider 
Y2K back in the 70s is a valid one, even if it isn't entirely fair.

As to your final point regarding investment, look at the millions of dollars 
that were paid by industry to purchase mainframes which didn't have a 
fraction of the power today's laptops have, followed by more millions 
maintaining infrastructure and specialists, to obtain applications that were 
just as shaky then as they are now, or just as robust then as they are now. 
Software quality does not depend entirely on investment. If you have crap 
programmers and pay above the market for them you will still get poor 
applications. If you underinvest in hardware you will get systems that don't 
perform as well as they could.

Strong, robust applications have been around for decades and so have flaky 
ones. I haven't seen anything to make me believe that what is being 
developed today will be any more or less robust than what was developed in 
the past. In fact, the general levels of knowledge today are better than 
they were in the last century so we might hope that things would actually 
improve.

People are more computer literate, languages are easier than they were (once 
you get over the ingrained procedural approach that most COBOL people have) 
and hardware is MUCH cheaper so it doesn't require the levels of investment 
that it once did, and, consequently, "computer programming" is more 
widespread. (Kids are teaching themselves scripting and programming in 
modern languages, simply downloading the tools for free off the Internet. It 
is a major growth "hobby"...)

As long as computers need people to program them (and that may not be for as 
long as many here believe) there will always be questions about quality. 
Human Beings are notoriously fallible, as well as being excellent.

As you point out, it is the artist, not the paintbrush

Pete.
```

Plural majestatus est, Mr Dashwood... I don't know who this 'we' you refer

(fairly common instruments in the USA) had year 2000 dates and higher 
after 2 Jan 1970.

Any time I addressed the Big Rollover (before 1996 or so) the response 
were predictable and included:

'The rest of the system isn't designed that way.'

'The system will have been retired long before 2000.'

'*I* won't be working here!'

... and, of course...

'The space costs too much.'

- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <OXZfk.17988$Ri.5145@flpi146.ffdc.sbc.com> <6ebmn9F6b9lrU1@mid.individual.net> <g5qe17$rr0$1@reader1.panix.com> <47fa165c-2508-4800-8e42-3ad75c49b415@r66g2000hsg.googlegroups.com>`

On Fri, 18 Jul 2008 12:29:31 -0700 (PDT), x01001x <xemail@softhome.net> wrote:

>
>> 'The system will have been retired long before 2000.'
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>outdated programs, and the functions of the out-dated programs.

C programs written in the 1980s could have used a six digit date, but the vast majority
did not. Why did Cobol programmers continue to use six digits, as late as systems
developed during the early 1990s, when other programmers did not?

I postulate the answer is cultural. They were following established norms. As others have
pointed out, limited memory was a rational reason for abbreviating dates in the 1960s. The
hardware limitation was largely gone in the 1970s, and completely gone by the 1980s. But
Cobol programmers in the 1980s already had 20 years of experience, whereas C programmers
were new to the game. That experience was etched in their style. 

They rationalized their choice of six digits by saying 'We'll all be gone by 2000' and
'This system won't last ten years' without serious thought or substantiation. Their
decision was based on style rather than reason. 

The culture of Cobol, unlike other languages, is oriented toward ritualistic conformance
with community norms, formalized as Shop Standards. Each shop acts as though its Standards
were engraved in granite and handed down from heaven, even though they wildly disagree
with each other (sounds like religion, doesn't it?) Beyond the published Standards are
many de facto stylistic norms saying 'do it the same way we've always done it and you'll
be safe.' The six digit date was one of those norms. 

In short, the Cobol culture was about conformity. Y2K was caused by 1960s hardware limits
projected to 1998 by cultural inertia.
```

I agree with you that the reasons for it were cultural, and it grieves me to 
also have to agree that much of the attitude you describe was  true at the

always done" as it was "Our existing files use 6 digits. Are you seriously 
suggesting we convert them all and that all new work should use 8?" In 1990 
that would have been a very hard position to hold and sell to Management 
Most sites were simply trapped by their legacy code and data.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-07-19T04:04:36+00:00

- **Message-ID:** `<oJdgk.274415$I42.237813@fe04.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <OXZfk.17988$Ri.5145@flpi146.ffdc.sbc.com> <6ebmn9F6b9lrU1@mid.individual.net> <g5qe17$rr0$1@reader1.panix.com> <47fa165c-2508-4800-8e42-3ad75c49b415@r66g2000hsg.googlegroups.com> <g9e2841u76dpq41ge3vh7iu464ut7bntse@4ax.com> <6ed51oF6js7qU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Robert and Pete,
  Using WHAT syntax would you have expected a program to get a 4-digit year in 
COBOL in the 1980's or early 1990's?

Of course the program COULD have "assumed" that "19" was the century, but 
STANDARD COBOL simply didn't have a way to get a 4-digit year until "FUNCTION 
CURRENT-DATE" was introduced in 1989 (ANSI) or 1991 (ISO) - and this wasn't 
implemented in many compilers until later in the 1990's.

Certainly the logic of
  if 2-digit-year > "50"
    Move "19" to CC-field
  Else
   Move "20" to CC-field
 end-if

was used (and probably exists in production COBOL code today.  (Some allow that 
"50" to be a moveable year)
```

```


"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote in message 
news:oJdgk.274415$I42.237813@fe04.news.easynews.com...
> Robert and Pete,
>  Using WHAT syntax would you have expected a program to get a 4-digit year 
> in COBOL in the 1980's or early 1990's?

The same way it would have done prior to that, and as in the example you 
posted. (You stated just ONE possibility... there are others. I liked the 
ones that used STRING... it was fairly unusual at the time...:-))

The point is that getting a four digit year was not a problem. It was 
storing it and subsequently using it that was problematic. If the record 
layout couldn't accommodate it without being changed you couldn't use it. If 
you wanted to change a record layout it required a committee, investigation, 
many compiles, regression testing, a ton of work that nobody really wanted 
to do.

If you suggested using four digit dates when new systems were being designed 
it was vetoed on the grounds of storage being expensive, and for all the 
other reasons that have been outlined here.

As year 2000 approached funds became available to do it (in fact, some 
people made large sums of money doing it... Quantas were one airline that 
spent $700 million dollars on it; I reckon they could have got it done for 
half that if it hadn't been hyped up...), and so it got done.

The bottom line is that culturally, the WILL to do it simply wasn't there. 
It took large sums of money to overcome the resistance.
>
> Of course the program COULD have "assumed" that "19" was the century, but 
> STANDARD COBOL simply didn't have a way to get a 4-digit year until 
> "FUNCTION CURRENT-DATE" was introduced in 1989 (ANSI) or 1991 (ISO) - and 
> this wasn't implemented in many compilers until later in the 1990's.

Didn't matter. We could have had four digit dates from 1959 if we had 
wanted.

We simply didn't want badly enough. If we had, we would have done it and 
swallowed the extra storage cost.

Actually, as events proved, it didn't matter anyway. When the need got dire 
enough to produce the funding, the job was done. A lot of IT works that way, 
even today...

>
> Certainly the logic of
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> that "50" to be a moveable year)

Yep. Seen it in many places.

Another little COBOL time bomb that will explode in  42 years time :-)  The 
MicroSoft one should explode about the same time, or, it WOULD do if nobody 
moved to 64 bit platforms. At least the MicroSoft one is automatically 
remedied by moving to 64 bit hardware, without the need for intervention in 
program code...

Of course, by then none of us will be writing code anyway and "computer 
programming" as we understand it will have the same relevance as lace making 
:-)

Pete.
 -- 
 "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."

"Richard" <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote in message

> MicroSoft one should explode about the same time, or, it WOULD do if

> program code...

[Pete]

Anyone silly enough to do that deserves whatever they get... :-)  You can 
hardly blame MS for application design stupidity. They have done their part, 
if the rest of us insist on using fields that are too small, that's hardly 
their problem.

[/Pete]

> Of course, by then none of us will be writing code anyway and "computer
> programming" as we understand it will have the same relevance as lace 
> making
> :-)

They said that in the 80s with 'The Last One' and even with LINK.

[Pete]

And in the ensuing 25 years huge progress has been made.

I haven't come across LINK (was that the Unisys package?) but did work on a 
site where they evaluated "The Last One".  It was decided not to pursue it. 
There was also some general laughter when it was succeeeded by "The Next 
One" some time later.

Nevertheless, it was a brave idea for the time. Most of us laugh at the old 
flickers of early flying machines collapsing on or just after take-off, but, 
without the failures, nothing would have been learned and we'd all still be 
using ships to visit distant lands. The Wright Brothers machine did not fly 
successfully the first time they tried it.

Personally, I don't believe computers will still be manually programmed by 
the middle of this century. It is only the arrogance of Human Beings that 
makes us think we are indispensable to such a process. It took us around 3 
million years and opposable thumbs to get to where we are with thought 
capacity and generalised problem solution capability. I believe we are 
capable of designing a computer that could certainly do it in half a 
century. Intense research is under way into deciphering the software of 
brains and incredible progress is being made. IBM can now simulate a fully 
functioning mouse brain; it was a step in our own evolution to move from 
such as that to what we have now. (It took around 70 million years...)

Your scepticism, while based on valid past experience, does not take into 
account the explosion of knowledge in general and the rapidly accellerating 
rate of knowledge acquisition and learning.  Ray Kurzweil (who DOES take 
these things into account and actually documents the increase in the rate we 
are going round the exponential curve) has 2048 as the date for his 
'singularity'. At this point humans merge with computers and programming is 
really irrelevant. We are then a combination of artifical and innate 
intelligence connected by a network, which presents information on request, 
just by thinking about it.

(There could only be learning and that would be at a rate we can't currently 
imagine. It might be interesting to find that you just "know" stuff but have 
no idea how you know it. (genetic and nano manipulations would provide it to 
your consciousness on request). I don't know whether he's right about this 
(he is right about a lot of things), and. maybe it will be a few hundred 
years before that happens, but it is certainly unlikely that the primitive 
way we currently use computers could continue on much after the middle of 
this century.)

Have a look at "The Singularity is near" by Ray Kurzweil. It has a very good 
bibliography and most of what is in it is not speculation. As a sometime 
computer programmer he writes in a way that can be appreciated by most 
people here.

Pete.

I have read of tape files of gigantic size (200 or more 2400' reels per
file) used by one of the US Government agencies - regrettably I can't
remember which one - and I'd bet that they haven't been converted (if in
fact they still exist).  I don't expect anything but that human beings will
remain the same - that by not remembering history they are condemned to
repeat it.  An application such as mortgage, where files are active for
decades, is probably going to be designed properly date-wise; but if there's
no reason to ENTER an 8-digit date then there's a chance that it won't be
stored as an 8-digit date.

Perhaps PD is right that in thirty years or so programming as we know it now
will no longer exist.  In that case we'd have to hope that there is an
inflexible requirement in the programming machines to require 8-digit date
(maybe even 10!  Who says this file won't exist in 10001C.E.??).  And we'd
no longer be worrying about it.  But there would still be a requirement for
meta-programmers - the ones who care & tend the programming machines - and
anybody who administers any programs will have to be aware of quite
intricate and low-level details - at least to the extent of recognizing
issues and knowing where to find the appropriate documentation.  As an
extreme example - it hasn't been determined yet if 10000C.E. will be a leap
year or not.  Hardly pressing, and yet when the day comes there's a good
chance that the programs will be wrong.

I can't actually see any point in time when all the necessary programming
has been done - which is a corollary of PD's prediction.

PL
```

>file) used by one of the US Government agencies - regrettably I can't
>remember which one - and I'd bet that they haven't been converted (if in
>fact they still exist).  

I don't think data tapes will exist in 2100.   Existing tapes won't be
readable.    It won't be for Y21C reasons that data will be migrated
off tapes and fiche onto databases, but they will be moved anyway.

>I don't expect anything but that human beings will
>remain the same - that by not remembering history they are condemned to
>repeat it.  

We tend to have similar, but not identical mistakes next time.   We
fight the last war, not the previous to the last war.

>An application such as mortgage, where files are active for
>decades, is probably going to be designed properly date-wise; but if there's
>no reason to ENTER an 8-digit date then there's a chance that it won't be
>stored as an 8-digit date.

Except that it's easier to tell the database that that number being
entered is a date.     We don't have to think, we don't have to work.

>Perhaps PD is right that in thirty years or so programming as we know it now
>will no longer exist.  In that case we'd have to hope that there is an
>inflexible requirement in the programming machines to require 8-digit date
>(maybe even 10!  Who says this file won't exist in 10001C.E.??).  

I bet you a beer that it won't exist then.

>And we'd
>no longer be worrying about it.  But there would still be a requirement for
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>chance that the programs will be wrong.

By then, if the human species still exists and computers still exist,
all the rules and methodologies will be different.

>I can't actually see any point in time when all the necessary programming
>has been done - which is a corollary of PD's prediction.
>
>PL
>

We will have different programming that will need to be done (maybe
not by humans) - but our old file based (CoBOL) systems will be
replaced by then.
```

process.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** SkippyPB <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com>
- **Date:** 2008-07-21T11:28:11-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<sfa984l0oq9g2j83ubfq0fme312o5km88g@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <OXZfk.17988$Ri.5145@flpi146.ffdc.sbc.com> <6ebmn9F6b9lrU1@mid.individual.net> <g5qe17$rr0$1@reader1.panix.com> <47fa165c-2508-4800-8e42-3ad75c49b415@r66g2000hsg.googlegroups.com> <g9e2841u76dpq41ge3vh7iu464ut7bntse@4ax.com> <8739849ocu7hos24g4bi5hi8tb0q751637@4ax.com>`

```
On Mon, 21 Jul 2008 07:21:11 -0600, Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
wrote:

>On Fri, 18 Jul 2008 20:28:16 -0500, Robert <no@e.mail> wrote:
>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>many systems of that era had six digit dates that were from the 1960s?

Mortgage systems written in the 1980s had to use 8 digit dates but
many other systems still used the 6 digit dates on the premise that
the software wasn't going to be around more than 10 years.

I rewrote 150+ programs comprising 5 different applications that were
written in the 1960's.  I did this in the late 70's.  The only reason
for the rewrite was the applications were going from IBM Assmebler to
IBM COBOL (Cobol-D if I recall).  No file changes were done or
required in terms of data stored.  Keyed files did go from DA to ISAM
but that was all.  All the date data fields remained 6 digits.

Regards,
          ////
         (o o)
-oOO--(_)--OOo-

"I went to a general store, but they wouldn't let me buy anything specific."
--Steven Wright
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

Remove nospam to email me.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-07-22T04:25:45+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cjdhk.23798$0O3.22116@fe02.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <OXZfk.17988$Ri.5145@flpi146.ffdc.sbc.com> <6ebmn9F6b9lrU1@mid.individual.net> <g5qe17$rr0$1@reader1.panix.com> <47fa165c-2508-4800-8e42-3ad75c49b415@r66g2000hsg.googlegroups.com> <g9e2841u76dpq41ge3vh7iu464ut7bntse@4ax.com> <8739849ocu7hos24g4bi5hi8tb0q751637@4ax.com> <sfa984l0oq9g2j83ubfq0fme312o5km88g@4ax.com>`

```
It seems to me that some of the discussion of 6-digit/8-digit dates (2 vs 4 
digit years) seems to be ignoring the issue of "dates in files" being a "who 
knows where a field is used problem".  In many large shops where dates had been 
stored with 2 digit years, the files were "databases" (in the most general sense 
of the word - and not related to whether they were "flat files" "indexed files" 
"hierarchical databases" "network databases" "relational databases" etc).  The 
problem was that many of these shops did not have good information on in which 
programs in which applications the fields were used.  This made it difficult to 
"fix" the storage of a field (change from 2-digit to 4-digit) years as "unknown 
side-effects" were feared.  Even when (after) it was determined which programs 
and applications would be impacted, it was often VERY difficult to change all 
the programs at the same time.

Obviously, this is what was required (in the end) but it was part of the reason 
for some shops delaying (postponing) such changes.

It also partially explained the rise in "repository" or "warehouse" gathering 
tools during the 90's.  (It also relates to the IBM - at least - introduction of 
tools to "recover" source code for lost programs.  This started with OS/VS COBOL 
to VS COBOL II conversions but became critical for some Y2K resolutions.)
```

archives: Computerworld, Datamation, etc., etc.: any computer magazine of
the time.  Lots of material there: you don't need us to cite it for you.

```
On Fri, 18 Jul 2008 05:08:47 -0500, "Michael Mattias"
<mmattias@talsystems.com> wrote:

>Of course, well-written software should have a long useful life; which 
>should tell you something vis-a-vis my opinion on today's quality versus the 
>quality we put into our products then. 

I don't see long-life is a requirement - unless there is a business
case for it.


I remember a few decades ago reading an article saying that schools
should not be built for a 100 year life span - because environments
change.    How many old hospitals have you been to with random
additions added to over the years, not designed for modern technology,
and not energy efficient?

Sometimes the best design is for a limited life-span.  Obviously when
the program is being used for one experiment or research project, but
often just because business cases change.

The problem is when a design is for a relatively short term, but there
is no budget to replace the system or product.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-07-18T22:00:05+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Fn8gk.255777$Ev5.40606@fe09.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <ga51849ep6mhrop5e9v9m1i5o4d13p5dkd@4ax.com> <a83dfecf-7073-4740-9508-b140a9d397dc@59g2000hsb.googlegroups.com>`

```
Almost all COBOL implementations are VERY concerned with "upward compatibility"

 - old source code needs to compile and run cleanly with new compilers
 - old object code need to run identically on new hardware and with new 
operating systems

Certainly, as much (possibly most) COBOL production code is (and was) running on 
IBM mainframes, *ALMOST EVERY* "well-written" program from the 1960's still runs 
correctly on the latest hardware and operating systems.  (A few "documented as 
no-no" things cause upward compatibility problems - and even few "old 
interfaces" have gone away.)

Although there are certainly MANY new things that business need computer 
programs to do, there are very few things that business needed done in 1965 and 
1975 and 1985 that they don't STILL need done.  If a program was designed "back 
then" to do it- and it did it correctly, why should it be replaced?  (There are 
reasons for replacing old applications, but they are FAR from universal.)
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
On Fri, 18 Jul 2008 22:00:05 GMT, "William M. Klein"
<wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:

>Almost all COBOL implementations are VERY concerned with "upward compatibility"
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>interfaces" have gone away.)

While you probably could run a batch COBOL program last compiled on
OS360 in the 1960's on a current IBM z series, CICS programs compiled
with VS COBOL will not run with current CICS (unless you have some
third party software).  Further because of differences in the standard
or IBM's implementation of certain things prior to them being
standardized, many programs written in the 1960's will either not
compile or compile and give different answers (even leaving out the
joys of compiler options change things).  I realize that you are aware
of and were probably overlooking the exceptions to make the point and
that COBOL can be far more consistent for some things within and
between platforms than C/C++.  Upward compatibility always has been
very important to the COBOL community.  The community understands the
joke about God being able to create the world in 7 days because there
was no installed base.  
>
>Although there are certainly MANY new things that business need computer 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>reasons for replacing old applications, but they are FAR from universal.)

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-07-18T21:04:54-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<2ei2845ue6dedoo4ro0m1cms9vv4jfh7n2@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <fl91845dmelqlf0oocmhtij6ujrvfneeo4@4ax.com> <33d97$4880b2fa$d0665605$11015@FUSE.NET> <a000ad87-b18b-44c0-b5c9-4dc9cd21b60e@b1g2000hsg.googlegroups.com> <rr8gk.282330$tY4.173565@fe06.news.easynews.com>`

```
On Fri, 18 Jul 2008 22:04:07 GMT, "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:

>Also, have you looked at how to get a 4-digit year in ANSI/ISO conforming COBOL 
>source code?  Have you looked at when these capabilities were added to each 
>COBOL compiler?

Cobol does not have a DATE type. The language standard does not specify whether dates are
stored as six or eight digits.  That's up to the application programmer. 

The ONLY place where the language standard is a factor is in getting the current date from
the operating system. Classically that's done with ACCEPT data-name FROM DATE. The 2002
(too late) standard added an option to get eight digits. In 1999 and 2000, the only
Standard-compliant value was six digits.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s - Y2K

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** Arnold Trembley <arnold.trembley@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2008-07-19T06:29:40+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<oRfgk.246339$SV4.37665@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<1Ldgk.253295$fz6.176478@fe08.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <fl91845dmelqlf0oocmhtij6ujrvfneeo4@4ax.com> <33d97$4880b2fa$d0665605$11015@FUSE.NET> <a000ad87-b18b-44c0-b5c9-4dc9cd21b60e@b1g2000hsg.googlegroups.com> <rr8gk.282330$tY4.173565@fe06.news.easynews.com> <2ei2845ue6dedoo4ro0m1cms9vv4jfh7n2@4ax.com> <1Ldgk.253295$fz6.176478@fe08.news.easynews.com>`

```
William M. Klein wrote:
> (see my other post)
> 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

As someone who worked on Y2K fixes, I can tell you the shop I worked 
in was still converting from OS/VS COBOL to COBOL II in 1996, and IBM 
COBOL II, as Bill Klein is well aware, did not support intrinsic 
functions.

Before 1998, if we needed to get current date with four-digit year, we 
would have had to write an assembler subprogram to get the date from 
MVS 5.2.2 and pass it to the COBOL calling program.  Around 1998, IBM 
supplied a COBOL II library routine, IGZEDT4, that would return the 
current date with four-digit year.

So "windowing" 2-digit years was a common and very cost effective 
solution.  Expanding all dates to use 4-digit years was impossible in 
certain situations, ACH file layouts for example (Automated Clearing 
House).  It would have been impossible to change the record layouts 
because there were millions of businesses using them to settle 
financial transactions.  How do you get all of them to agree on how 
and when to convert their file formats?

And there were plenty of problems with C/C++ programs.  It was quite 
common to see 991231 rollover to 1000101, or the even more perverse 
rollover of 1999-12-31 to 19100-01-01 or even 1910-01-01.

There was even an entire usenet news group devoted to the problem - 
comp.software.year-2000.  Docdwarf will remember how much fun that 
was.  It can still be searched using Google groups.

For me the most interesting thing was the number of people who eagerly 
anticipated the catastrophic failure of all the computers on the planet.

Say Doc, do you remember that InfoMagic guy, the one who predicted 
that it would be 300 years after the rollover before we would have 
electricity again?
```

```
Robert wrote:
> On Sat, 19 Jul 2008 06:29:40 GMT, Arnold Trembley <arnold.trembley@worldnet.att.net>
> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Very simple. Put a format Version Number near the beginning of the record. 

Technically, this solution would work.  But in practice it would fail, 
because some very large and important customer would refuse to upgrade 
their systems to support the new ACH format.  Senders of ACH files 
would have to prepare files in either old or new format, depending 
upon who would be receiving them.  There was no way to force every 
user of ACH files to support new record formats by a specific date.

And record layout changes weren't needed.  ACH financial transactions 
are so transitory that date windowing was a perfectly workable 
solution.  Keeping the existing record layouts saved a considerable 
amount of money in development costs.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-07-19T06:33:15-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9oh384ls5696p2f2jtgnajug61aq808l0u@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <fl91845dmelqlf0oocmhtij6ujrvfneeo4@4ax.com> <33d97$4880b2fa$d0665605$11015@FUSE.NET> <a000ad87-b18b-44c0-b5c9-4dc9cd21b60e@b1g2000hsg.googlegroups.com> <rr8gk.282330$tY4.173565@fe06.news.easynews.com> <2ei2845ue6dedoo4ro0m1cms9vv4jfh7n2@4ax.com> <1Ldgk.253295$fz6.176478@fe08.news.easynews.com>`

```
On Sat, 19 Jul 2008 04:06:22 GMT, "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:

>(see my other post)
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>Accept from Day/Date <YYYYMMDD/YYYYYDDD) was later

Thanks for the information, but it misses the point. The Y2K crisis was caused by six
digit dates in files, not by the resolution of the system date. The Cobol language doesn't
tell you how to store a date in a file. 

There was no crisis in applications that used databases because databases have a date type
as a fundamental data type. Cobol doesn't, it offers strings and several types of numbers.
Application programmers usually stored a date as a six byte string or six digit number.
They could have used eight digits.

The choice of six rather than eight may have been influenced by hardware. Non-IBM CPUs
favored the 32-bit integer, which has room for nine decimal digits. Six digits did not
take less space. I postulate that's why C programmers used eight digit dates. IBM CPUs
favored packed decimal, wherein every two decimal digits took an additional byte. A six
digit date took four bytes, which was a nice round number in terms of memory boundaries;
eight digits took five bytes, which portended to produce 'slack bytes' for alignment.
Thus, mainframe programmers were nudged to think six digits were harmonious with the
hardware whereas eight digits were potentially problematic.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()

```

>Application programmers usually stored a date as a six byte string or
>six digit number.
>They could have used eight digits.

In my experience, Mr Wagner, if applications programmers stored dates in 
eight digits where the person signing the checks for the project demanded 
six digits then those applications programmers would have been looking for 
new jobs.

>
>The choice of six rather than eight may have been influenced by
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>take less space.

The CPU is not the only consideration for space, Mr Wagner.

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-07-19T14:12:45-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1hd484ln2a0vn7kcgrc6csq6l0v04dlfed@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <2ei2845ue6dedoo4ro0m1cms9vv4jfh7n2@4ax.com> <1Ldgk.253295$fz6.176478@fe08.news.easynews.com> <9oh384ls5696p2f2jtgnajug61aq808l0u@4ax.com> <g5t1p2$3rr$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
On Sat, 19 Jul 2008 15:38:10 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>In article <9oh384ls5696p2f2jtgnajug61aq808l0u@4ax.com>,
>Robert  <no@e.mail> wrote:
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>dates in which years were expressed in two digits.

Date arithmetic cannot be performed (directly) on six or eight digit dates. It requires
conversion to Lilian, Julian or similar form. Bad programmers got that wrong too. They
abrogated the phrase Julian Date to mean YYDDD (day of year), when the phrase already had
a more useful meaning. YYDDD was a stupid choice for arithmetic. 

I used what I called Century Date, whose floor was 0 Jan 1900. Now I see it called Dublin
Julian Day, invented by the International Astronomical Union in 1955. It is also called
the Excel serial date. 

Cobol's integer date begins 1/01/1601, which makes no sense. If they wanted to go that far
back, they should have used the beginning of the Gregorian calendar in 1582. 


>Whether those dates were in files, passed parms or pulled out of a 
>magician's hat did not change the arithmetic underpinnings.
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>new jobs.

Help me out, I'm having trouble picturing a hard-headed Business Executive pounding a
conference table and loudly demanding six digit dates rather than eight, glaring at anyone
who disagreed.
```

```


"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
news:std58411sf6rtd2c99r4hnbp0a6b5rqfpk@4ax.com...
> On Sat, 19 Jul 2008 23:42:19 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:
>
…[65 more quoted lines elided]…
> packages such as SAP and Peoplesoft.

There is truth in what you are saying, but it is not the whole truth. 
Certainly, most executives were unconcerned about the size of date fields in 
COBOL programs, quite properly delegating this to the people who were 
supposed to know. However, once it was raised as a potential problem there 
was a lot of yelling and screaming and running around in circles, where 
there should have been some considered guidance and leadership. I remember 
talking to some executives at a major corporation in Sydney and being 
apalled at their lack of understanding of the problem, their readiness to 
punish the innocent, and their complete lack of any coherent plan for 
dealing with the situation. I was offered a substantial sum of money to 
"sort it out" for them, but declined. I heard later they got Arthur Andersen 
in, so I shudder to think what their systems are like nowadays... :-)

The truth is that there was culpability at all levels: Management, 
Technical, even Operational. Certainly the COBOL culture didn't help as you 
pointed out previously, but once the problem became apparent, there were 
many less than perfect solutions.

But assigning blame achieves nothing (unless positive lessons are learned to 
prevent mistakes being repeated, and with Y2K there wasn't a lot of that 
going on), and it definitely demotivates people.

Some places were moving to packages and SAP before Y2K. It wasn't 
disillusionment with COBOL, rather, the promise of a more cost/effective 
solution.

> Y2K was an unrecoverable loss of confidence in Cobol culture .. the kiss 
> of death.

I don't think so. It may have been a contributing factor, but it would have 
been a small one. It was the rejection of OO COBOL by the user base in the 
mid/late 90s which was the kiss of death for COBOL. Had it been embraced as 
an OO language,  had the TCO been much less, and had installations 
modernised themselves and their developmental approaches to implement it, we 
would see a different world today. I believe the culture was largely 
responsible for that, along with a degree of inertia and smugness in some 
quarters.

> Management will never trust its in-house IT Establishment as it did before 
> Y2K. The crisis
> taught management that better ideas were available outside.

Probably, to some extent.

I think Management DO trust their in-house IT departments but only as long 
as they are delivering results. When the business has to go cap-in-hand to 
IT and suffer humiliation and embarrassment from tech staff, when IT staff 
see the company's investment in hardware and software as a means to add new 
letters to their CV, rather than facilitating service the delivery of 
service by them, when the culture is one of  limited vision and 
traditionalism, then the IT department's days may be numbered. There was a 
time when the organization had no choice; they simply had to suffer it. Now 
they don't. The broadening of computer literacy and the computer skill base, 
the improvements in hardware and software technology, the cheaper TCO in 
alternatives to COBOL, have all had a major effect. It wasn't just Y2K.

Pete.
```

I can "hear" joke when I read it, but, in case anyone really DOESN'T know why 
January 1, 1601 was chosen, it was because this supported the DAY-OF-WEEK 
integer being the "MOD 7" value of the pseudo-Lillian date (ignoring "0" instead 
of "7").

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Joel C. Ewing" <jcREMOVEewing@CAPS.acm.org>
- **Date:** 2008-07-20T17:07:26-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Ob2dnTNueZm_Kh7VnZ2dnUVZ_q7inZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<1hd484ln2a0vn7kcgrc6csq6l0v04dlfed@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <2ei2845ue6dedoo4ro0m1cms9vv4jfh7n2@4ax.com> <1Ldgk.253295$fz6.176478@fe08.news.easynews.com> <9oh384ls5696p2f2jtgnajug61aq808l0u@4ax.com> <g5t1p2$3rr$1@reader1.panix.com> <1hd484ln2a0vn7kcgrc6csq6l0v04dlfed@4ax.com>`

```
Robert wrote:
> On Sat, 19 Jul 2008 15:38:10 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:
> 
…[49 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

The Y2K problem, more accurately, was caused by the use of fewer than 4 
digits for the year in programs and data files in contexts where the 
precise date became ambiguous across a century boundary.  Finding the 
difference between two dates is just one instance where the ambiguity 
becomes obvious. If the date is supposed to accurately record an 
historical or future event and it's ambiguous, you have a problem even 
if date differences are not explicitly calculated.

Those of the latest generation who have never bothered to study the 
history of computing just have no concept of the limited resources that 
were available during the early days of business computing.  Those whose 
experience is limited to the post-MS Windows era have probably never 
even heard of a computer with less than 4 MiB of memory (make that 128 
MiB if post-Win-XP).  Many early business computers in the 1950's had 4 
KiB or less!!! It probably wasn't until the late 1970's that machines 
with as much as 1 MiB of memory became affordable for most businesses. 
Input data records were constrained by 80-character card images.  Other 
external data storage devices were pricey and equally constrained. 
Programmers had to  employ many tricks and short-cuts in order to 
implement applications within the hardware and budgetary constraints 
imposed, and abbreviated year formats were only one of those devices. 
In those early days no one had any concept that data and programs from 
those early applications would still be around for 10 years, much less 
40 - in fact, prior to the IBM S/360 hardware announcement in 1964, 
migrating applications to new hardware typically meant a major re-write 
of all applications would occur in less than 10 years, so it was not 
unreasonable for early programmers to expect that to continue.

That the mindset of programmers for efficient date storage in some cases 
lasted beyond the point when more weight should have been given to the 
ambiguity issues implied by the century rollover is obvious; but then 
the optimum use of manpower and hardware resources from a management 
standpoint would be to resolve these issues at the least cost - which 
for some businesses could very well have been at the last possible 
minute.  Making changes to applications that increases operational costs 
and provides benefits only in the distant future is always a hard sell 
to a typical manager.

To imply that early programmers are to blame for inventing two-digit 
year formats is inaccurate.  Use of this format was common for manually 
recorded dates long before the present computer era.  Its use in early 
computer applications could just as easily been a requirement from the 
application's end-users.  The trick was that humans reading such dates 
[generally] understood the context and had no trouble deducing the true 
date.

Aside on the IBM Julian date:
IBM hardly abrogated (=repealed) the astronomical definition of "Julian 
Day" (which, I believe, is more correctly referred to as "Julian Day", 
not "Julian date"), as that astronomical definition is still in use by 
astronomers -- and probably few others, since those of us who are 
awake/working during the day find the concept of changing the date at 
noon singularly non-useful.  IBM simply overloaded "Julian" to have a 
different meaning in the context of computer dates.  Giving words 
different meanings and implications in different contexts and different 
fields of endeavor is a highly common practice.  There is no reason why 
the interpretation of "Julian" in "Julian day" should be sacrosanct -- 
any more than the use of "Julian Day" by astronomers be criticized for 
use of "Julian" with a totally different meaning from that in "Julian 
Calendar", which preceded the astronomical term by more than 15 
centuries.  One can perhaps deduce that there were few of astronomical 
bent at IBM in the early days of computing, or a different name might 
have been chosen.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

_(thread depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-07-21T11:34:36+12:00

```
Joel C. Ewing wrote:
[snipped]
> The Y2K problem, more accurately, was caused by the use of fewer than 4 
> digits for the year in programs and data files in contexts where the 
…[61 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

The DEC VAX 11/780 and it's operating system, VAX/VMS (currently
OpenVMS) were designed in concert during the late 1970s. The system's
time format was designed as a 64 bit integer holding a time value
(count) representing the number of clock ticks of a 10 MHz clock (100
nanosecond resolution) accumulated since November 17, 1858.

Nov 17, 1858 is modified Julian day 2,400,000. See

   <http://vms.tuwien.ac.at/info/humour/vms-base-time-origin.txt>

for a quick overview.

It's a shame other other engineering groups didn't think the problem
through as thoroughly.

Jeff

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Joel C. Ewing" <jcREMOVEewing@CAPS.acm.org>
- **Date:** 2008-07-21T23:18:23-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<xZOdnYC5gp4LwhjVnZ2dnUVZ_tvinZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<1216624726_13381@isp.n>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <2ei2845ue6dedoo4ro0m1cms9vv4jfh7n2@4ax.com> <1Ldgk.253295$fz6.176478@fe08.news.easynews.com> <9oh384ls5696p2f2jtgnajug61aq808l0u@4ax.com> <g5t1p2$3rr$1@reader1.panix.com> <1hd484ln2a0vn7kcgrc6csq6l0v04dlfed@4ax.com> <Ob2dnTNueZm_Kh7VnZ2dnUVZ_q7inZ2d@earthlink.com> <1216624726_13381@isp.n>`

```
Jeff Campbell wrote:
...
> 
> The DEC VAX 11/780 and it's operating system, VAX/VMS (currently
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> Jeff

I think they did.

IBM's S/370 in the early 1970 (when memory and and hardware were a 
little more expensive and slower) was designed with a 56-bit Time-of-Day 
clock containing microseconds count with origin at 0000 Jan 1, 1900 and 
a cycle length of approximately 134 years, and this was the basis for 
all date-time values supplied by Operating Systems on that architecture. 
  It has since been supplemented by an Extended TOD hardware value 
(128-bit) with higher resolution and longer cycle for the rare cases 
where raw TOD values are used for a time stamp, which is typically only 
within IBM code and system data structures, very rarely in application 
code or application defined data fields.  Some of the MVS date formats 
at the time that had abbreviated years values had to be left that way 
for compatibility with earlier hardware and operating systems.  I 
suspect the DEC VAX design was was not subject to such compatibility 
constraints.

It doesn't really matter what the hardware and system internal time 
stamp format is as long as the system can supply time to an appropriate 
accuracy and supply correct dates in more conventional date formats. 
Applications are of necessity forced to use date-time constructs that 
are familiar to the end-users in external user interfaces, not some 
hardware-specific counter.  This forces those conventional date formats 
to be in some parts of the applications internals, and can also result 
in those date formats finding their way into external files.  Once you 
have conventional date formats flying around, you open up the same 
opportunity for abbreviated year fields and potentially ambiguous dates 
in user interfaces, application code, and files - so having an internal 
hardware/system date-time representation that minimizes ambiguity is 
unfortunately no guarantee that applications on that system would be 
immune from date ambiguity issues.  And conversely, having less robust 
hardware/system time stamp support doesn't prevent an intelligent 
application design that avoids date ambiguity problems.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

_(thread depth: 13)_

- **From:** "robertwessel2@yahoo.com" <robertwessel2@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2008-07-22T16:36:03-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ac7e9a4c-a80f-4631-b5bb-2e6770d754f1@w7g2000hsa.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <2ei2845ue6dedoo4ro0m1cms9vv4jfh7n2@4ax.com> <1Ldgk.253295$fz6.176478@fe08.news.easynews.com> <9oh384ls5696p2f2jtgnajug61aq808l0u@4ax.com> <g5t1p2$3rr$1@reader1.panix.com> <1hd484ln2a0vn7kcgrc6csq6l0v04dlfed@4ax.com> <Ob2dnTNueZm_Kh7VnZ2dnUVZ_q7inZ2d@earthlink.com> <1216624726_13381@isp.n> <xZOdnYC5gp4LwhjVnZ2dnUVZ_tvinZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
On Jul 21, 11:18 pm, "Joel C. Ewing" <jcREMOVEew...@CAPS.acm.org>
wrote:
> IBM's S/370 in the early 1970 (when memory and and hardware were a
> little more expensive and slower) was designed with a 56-bit Time-of-Day
> clock containing microseconds count with origin at 0000 Jan 1, 1900 and
> a cycle length of approximately 134 years,


Actually the old TOD clock was 64 bits, defined as (apparently)
incrementing once each microsecond in the 52nd bit (counting from the
high end).  Equivalently, the least significant bit incremented 4096
times each microsecond.  Actual implementations picked a spot to
increment based on the speed of a processor (so a slow processor might
have incremented the 50th bit every four microseconds, a fast one bit
54 every quarter microsecond) , but the overall rate for the counter
was unchanged.

The 1/1/1900 base is a convention, and not really dictated by the
hardware (until much more recently, where time synchronization got
added to the hardware, which sort-of forces the issue), but was
universally implemented by IBM (and other) OS's.

The extended (128 bit) version adds bits to both ends of the old
value, dealing with the rollover in 2043, as well as with processors
executing more than a few billion instructions per second.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-07-21T22:24:53-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<p9ga84dhdve347e3sks6qdcfvfnme0bppc@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <fl91845dmelqlf0oocmhtij6ujrvfneeo4@4ax.com> <33d97$4880b2fa$d0665605$11015@FUSE.NET> <a000ad87-b18b-44c0-b5c9-4dc9cd21b60e@b1g2000hsg.googlegroups.com> <rr8gk.282330$tY4.173565@fe06.news.easynews.com> <2ei2845ue6dedoo4ro0m1cms9vv4jfh7n2@4ax.com> <1Ldgk.253295$fz6.176478@fe08.news.easynews.com> <9oh384ls5696p2f2jtgnajug61aq808l0u@4ax.com> <3e70001f-0513-44b4-a6c5-dc6ad9e44cb4@b2g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Mon, 21 Jul 2008 13:14:35 -0700 (PDT), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Jul 19, 11:33ï¿½pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
>> On Sat, 19 Jul 2008 04:06:22 GMT, "William M. Klein" <wmkl...@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
>> as a fundamental data type. Cobol doesn't, it offers strings and several types of numbers.

My point was Y2K wouldn't have been much of a crisis if Cobol had a date type, a
reasonable expectation in a Business Language.

>It does give a 6 character date from ACCEPT xxx FROM DATE. This then
>was the default COBOL date format.

Whatever the reason, the Cobol community adopted six characters as a  norm and stuck with
it long after the memory shortage was over, BECAUSE it was a 'standard practice.'

>> Application programmers usually stored a date as a six byte string or six digit number.
>> They could have used eight digits.
>
>You are not suggesting that binary be stored in data files are you ?

That was common in the 60s and 70s. Some stored telephone numbers, social security numbers
and postal codes as packed or binary. Postal codes were especially short-signteed because
non-US codes contain alpha. 

The same people saw nothing wrong with padding strings with spaces, and adding 100 bytes
at the end of a record for future expansion. An average 4x30 name and address might waste
60 bytes with trailing spaces, because there was there was no convenient way to make
strings variable length in Cobol. There was in PL/I.

When disk space was expensive, in the late 60s, I wrote a program that analyzed disk
utilization. When I ran it on medium sized mainframes, it usually found 10% of the space
contained information, 90% was wasted due to fixed length fields, records, blocks, and
disk partitions, which occurred at several levels. The computer (and disk)  manufacturer
had no incentive to help customers save space. The more waste, the more money it made.
Third party disk space management software, such as DYNAM-D, was created to solve this
problem. 

It is hard to believe that some early disks had no interleave. Blocks were arranged
sequentially around a track. Sequential reads took a full rotation for each block. A
simple interleave optimizer I wrote at Honeywell made most disk-bound programs run 2-4
times as fast. The company had no interest in releasing it.  Modern disks again have no
interleave, because they have buffers bigger than a track.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-07-21T21:44:43-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<be231a03-bc2a-4c62-ae91-ecd9e9bbbedf@u12g2000prd.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <fl91845dmelqlf0oocmhtij6ujrvfneeo4@4ax.com> <33d97$4880b2fa$d0665605$11015@FUSE.NET> <a000ad87-b18b-44c0-b5c9-4dc9cd21b60e@b1g2000hsg.googlegroups.com> <rr8gk.282330$tY4.173565@fe06.news.easynews.com> <2ei2845ue6dedoo4ro0m1cms9vv4jfh7n2@4ax.com> <1Ldgk.253295$fz6.176478@fe08.news.easynews.com> <9oh384ls5696p2f2jtgnajug61aq808l0u@4ax.com> <3e70001f-0513-44b4-a6c5-dc6ad9e44cb4@b2g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <p9ga84dhdve347e3sks6qdcfvfnme0bppc@4ax.com>`

```
On Jul 22, 3:24 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
> On Mon, 21 Jul 2008 13:14:35 -0700 (PDT), Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:
> >On Jul 19, 11:33 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
…[55 more quoted lines elided]…
> sequentially around a track. Sequential reads took a full rotation for each block.

Nonsense. Drums in the 1950s had interleave so the technique was well
established. All the hard disks that I worked with in the 60s and 70s
not only had interleave but we optimised the file access by pre-
allocating some files in blocks using the same seek areas. This
allowed a sequential file-update to be done with minimum head moves.

In very early computers with mercury tube memory access was so slow
that programs were laid out in memory to minimise latency. Several
instructions could execute in one cycle, each instruction had a 'next
instruction address' that would be the right distance down the memory
tube to collect when required.

> A
> simple interleave optimizer I wrote at Honeywell made most disk-bound programs run 2-4
> times as fast. The company had no interest in releasing it.

I've used interleave optimisers. It wasn't that the disk wasn't
interleaved, but that changing the interleave could, for particular
patterns of access, could make the system run faster. This depended on
whether the processing of the data was completed in time to catch the
next block. If the interleave was, say, 2, then processing time may
mean that the next block is just missed. Changing to 3 would give
ample time and mean less missed revolutions.

Of course for other operations this may well slow down the total
thruput. In the above example for a process that could get the next
read request up quickly enough the change to interleave 3 would mean 3
revs per track instead of 2, slowing it down 50%.

Given that the company wants the fastest performance figures for its
disks then your program may speed up real work but it would give worse
benchmark results (and worse results on some real work too).

In the 70s I did much work with Queuing Theory to calculate
interactive system performance targets and this counted revolutions
and latency as well as instruction estimates.

>  Modern disks again have no
> interleave, because they have buffers bigger than a track.

"""Understanding interleaving is still important because the concept
forms the basis for more advanced techniques such as head and cylinder
skew, which are used on modern drives."""

It should say _still_ used. The 2900 Series controllers used skew in
the 70s. It also used position sensing. That is, instead of waiting
for a particular sector that was next in the queue it would complete
the head move, calculate what the next sector was available was and
see if that was required by _any_ request in the queue.

This meant that with multi-tasking each programs could request several
blocks (a new program segment for example) and the controller would
read the sectors in whatever order was fastest to fulfill the
requests. It would also do elevator seeks to optimise the head moves.

Interleaving as a tuning technique is only useful for single tasking
machines. Actually we can't tell if interleaving is still used. IDE,
SCSI and SATA drives have the controllers on-board. They may well
interleave and cylinder skew. For some drives it might take 2 or 3
revolutions to fill the track buffer. They may even do it with head
switching, so that sequential sectors actually come from different
surfaces. The aim of the manufacturer is to have the biggest numbers
for their benchmark tests. Just like Honeywell they don't want people
like you changing their performance figures, even if it does speed up
_some_ stuff.
```

```
On Mon, 21 Jul 2008 21:44:43 -0700 (PDT), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Jul 22, 3:24ï¿½pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:

>> It is hard to believe that some early disks had no interleave. Blocks were arranged
>> sequentially around a track. Sequential reads took a full rotation for each block.
>
>Nonsense.

It's not nonsense, it was a fact.

>Drums in the 1950s had interleave so the technique was well
>established.

Honeywell never had drums.

> All the hard disks that I worked with in the 60s and 70s
>not only had interleave but we optimised the file access by pre-
>allocating some files in blocks using the same seek areas. This
>allowed a sequential file-update to be done with minimum head moves.

Putting each file on a separate drive produced even less head movement.

Burroughs had head-per-track disks whose heads didn't move.

>In very early computers with mercury tube memory access was so slow
>that programs were laid out in memory to minimise latency. Several
>instructions could execute in one cycle, each instruction had a 'next
>instruction address' that would be the right distance down the memory
>tube to collect when required.

The IBM 650 was like that. It had memory for the current instruction and a few registers.
The program resided on a drum. After compiling, one ran a program named SOAP, which
distributed instructions around the drum based on their execution speed. 

>> A
>> simple interleave optimizer I wrote at Honeywell made most disk-bound programs run 2-4
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>ample time and mean less missed revolutions.

Right. My optimizer used interleave 2 on executable programs, because the loader was fast,
interleave 3 on small files and interleave half a track on large files. 

>Of course for other operations this may well slow down the total
>thruput. In the above example for a process that could get the next
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>and latency as well as instruction estimates.

I worked on a computer simulator named SCERT by Comress. Its main use was configuring a
mainframe to run a system that was not written yet. 

>> ï¿½Modern disks again have no
>> interleave, because they have buffers bigger than a track.
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>see if that was required by _any_ request in the queue.

IBM had a refrigerator-sized array controller that did those calculations. 

Now they run Tivoli software, which sucks whole files into memory.

>This meant that with multi-tasking each programs could request several
>blocks (a new program segment for example) and the controller would
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>_some_ stuff.

Honeywell simply didn't understand what interleaving was about. It didn't understand many
things, which is why it got out of the computer business. In 1999, it was acquired by
AlliedSignal, which changed its name to Honeywell.
```

On Jul 23, 1:13 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
> On Mon, 21 Jul 2008 21:44:43 -0700 (PDT), Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>
> >I've used interleave optimisers. It wasn't that the disk wasn't
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Then, it seems, you weren't interleaving the disk, but were
distributing the allocation blocks for a file. Interleaving is a fixed
parameter for a disk, it is set when the disk is formatted, and can't
be varied by file.

Certainly a file can have its allocated blocks manipulated. DOS/
Windows de-fragging tries to move all the clusters to being contiguous
to save head moves and FAT searches. It seems that you 'fragged' the
clusters to give an emulation of interleaving.
```

```
On Jul 21, 10:24 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
> That was common in the 60s and 70s. Some stored telephone numbers, social security numbers
> and postal codes as packed or binary. Postal codes were especially short-signteed because
> non-US codes contain alpha.


Semi-entertainingly, the fact that many shops stored 5 digit US postal
(ZIP) codes in display decimal format eased their conversion process
when the USPS introduced the extended ZIP+4 codes in the early
eighties.  The nine digit ZIP+4 fit neatly into the same five bytes,
if you changed to format to packed.  I even saw some places build
logic to try and validate the field both ways, to tell what was in it
(since no valid 5 byte display decimal number is a valid five byte
packed number, and vice-versa).

Nothing like a Q&D hack to get around a problem...
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<lg5c84lphj1q7ml4a3k2enhnll7gv73gu1@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <fl91845dmelqlf0oocmhtij6ujrvfneeo4@4ax.com> <33d97$4880b2fa$d0665605$11015@FUSE.NET> <a000ad87-b18b-44c0-b5c9-4dc9cd21b60e@b1g2000hsg.googlegroups.com> <rr8gk.282330$tY4.173565@fe06.news.easynews.com> <2ei2845ue6dedoo4ro0m1cms9vv4jfh7n2@4ax.com> <1Ldgk.253295$fz6.176478@fe08.news.easynews.com> <9oh384ls5696p2f2jtgnajug61aq808l0u@4ax.com>`

```
On Sat, 19 Jul 2008 06:33:15 -0500, Robert <no@e.mail> wrote:

>On Sat, 19 Jul 2008 04:06:22 GMT, "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:
>
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
>hardware whereas eight digits were potentially problematic. 

What slack bytes?  On the IBM 360/370/390/z we normally did not use
binary for fields on files, there would not have been any slack bytes
needed.  I suspect that continuing with 2 digit years had more to do
with the following constraints:

1.  The original files were set up with 2 digit years and it would be
to expensive to change and consistency was important.  

2.  For many reports, the detail line was severely constrained so
keeping the amount of space required for a field to a minimum was
important.

3.  Similarly many data entry screens were space constrained.
```

```


"x01001x" <xemail@softhome.net> wrote in message 
news:a000ad87-b18b-44c0-b5c9-4dc9cd21b60e@b1g2000hsg.googlegroups.com...
>
>> It's just hard to conceive by
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> purpose was broken.

Do you usually come to a conclusion this early when doing "research"? A 
number of people have posted telling you that the programs were NOT 
technically insufficient, yet you have already decided this is not true.


>So why were these programs not fixed around 1994
> when it was clearly possible to do so.

Because they weren't broken. If Y2K had not occurred, they would have 
continued running exactly as usual.

I have been in this game since 1965 and have worked all over the world in 
varied technical environments on different platforms. I have yet to see any 
place were "broken" programs were put into production and left there for any 
period of time. (Users tend to become annoyed when things don't work and 
they make the most dreadful fuss about it... very tiresome really... :-))

There is a case which says that ALL computer programs written by Humans are 
"broken" but many just haven't crashed yet... However, we have to operate on 
the asumption that production programs, for all intents and purposes, are 
NOT broken.

Given that people knew the year 2000 was approaching, and given that they 
also knew that there would be problems with date fields being too short, you 
could ask why some pre-emptive action wasn't taken... The answer is that it 
was. Your quibble seems to be about WHEN it was taken.

No planes fell out of the sky, no nuclear missiles were launched, no 
telephone systems closed down, invoices continued to be printed, dated and 
sent, the whole thing, at least for the general public, was a damp firework.

And yet thousands of IT staff worked for thousands of man hours hours to 
ensure it was safe. It wouldn't make any difference to the end result 
whether those thousands of man hours were put in in 1995 or 1999. The job 
was done.

Perhaps the effort deserves praise rather than castigation?

> Why were these companies using old COBOL code all the way until the
> last minute?

Because it did the job. (You seem to be having difficulty with the 
concept...:-))


> Procrastination is a terrible vice.
>

Hamlet didn't think so. (Admittedly he came to a sticky end...).

Me, I'm not so bothered by it... in fact I've put off worrying about it. :-)

So far, my world hasn't collapsed.

Taiichi Ohno at Toyota made a lifetime study of it and founded the JIT 
inventory system. Hardly a bad thing. The .NET CLR uses the same principle, 
along with a number of modern scripting languages...

Leaving things until the last minute is a winning strategy in a number of 
fields.


Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

- **From:** billg999@cs.uofs.edu (Bill Gunshannon)
- **Date:** 2008-07-19T13:59:26+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6eea9uF6omp7U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <fl91845dmelqlf0oocmhtij6ujrvfneeo4@4ax.com> <33d97$4880b2fa$d0665605$11015@FUSE.NET>`

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

```
Probably, at least half of the
 - utility bills
 - insurance bills
 - bank statements

that you receive TODAY include a large portion of their "back-end" processing in 
COBOL - and if the company is over 30 years old, then the chances are MUCH of 
the code was written in COBOL in the '60's '70's, and '80's.

Although your monthly bills and statements may be online now and use lots of 
"pretty fonts" - the chances are that the INFORMATION is the same as was placed 
on bills in past decades.

Why change the BUSINESS logic - if hasn't changed.  (COBOL was particularly good 
at handling "decimal currency" issues at a time when many other languages could 
do so only with a lot of effort.)  Again, the business logic hasn't changed, so 
why should the program change?

  ***

Even when you get to "user interfaces' for today's GUI and web-based 
applications (many - certainly well LESS than half) of those have CICS or 
similar "character based" back-ends that are still doing the "data processing" 
(but not the interface) portions of the application.
```

#### ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2008-07-18T08:37:30-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6ba184tve1i8io4ikfbv750khsdqiovo1v@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Thu, 17 Jul 2008 23:26:26 -0700 (PDT), x01001x
<xemail@softhome.net> wrote:

>So I guess it was around 1998-1999 I started hearing all this stuff
>about how the old COBOL programs needed to be Y2k-upgraded, because
>they were only able to handle two-digit dates instead of 4-digit
>dates.

The problem wasn't about CoBOL, it was about data.    Back when
storing data was relatively more expensive, it was cost effective to
store dates without including the century.    As the new century got
shorter, we needed to compare dates from before and after the year
2000.   Fortunately, the cost of storing the larger date was way
cheaper by then, but unfortunately, that meant converting all of the
old data to a larger date - and converting all of the programs that
accessed the old data to the adjusted format.    

Since most programs that accessed those old files were written in
CoBOL, most of those changes were done by CoBOL programmers.
```

big issue of it, but it was just part of the process of updating
systems.

In many cases other languages were just as vulnerable to Y2K, though
in many cases different problems. C programs that did: printf("%02d/
%02d/%02d", d.tm_day, d.tm_mon+1, d.tm_yr); produced 01/01/100 on 1st
January 2000.

Many systems will already have an issue with 2038 dates, especially
those where mortgages are issued for 30 years.

Much software being written today does not cater for this problem,
saying that it '30 years away' is _exactly_ what happened in the 1970s
about Y2K.

Why do you think that code from the 80s is 'ancient' ? Perhaps you
have never written any code that is worth keeping around and toss it
out after a few days.

> What kind of hardware would even still be heavily used at that time
> which would support old COBOL code?

Almost all desktop or server hardware produced 10 years ago or today
is capable of running COBOL code. It is just another language, one
particularly good at doing the tasks it is designed for.

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-07-20T19:45:26-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<plh784d4ni0suta2h3enei1v5b5p28q7gg@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<b1c5d257-8c79-43a8-a8d0-e986530039ac@d19g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <ZhNgk.3637$W64.1662@newsfe09.iad>`

```
On Sun, 20 Jul 2008 15:33:14 -0500, "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote:

>
>x01001x <xemail@softhome.net> wrote in message
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>complex.  Anybody know more of these?

 Burroughs Medium Systems was a line of Cobol computers. Most Cobol statements compiled to
one machine instruction. Most machine instructions had two operands where each operand had
type, length and address. If you wanted to add a packed number to a display number, you
could do it with one instruction. It had two move and compare instructions: alpha and
numeric. When sending and receiving fields were of unequal length, the numeric ones added
or deleted leading zeros (and did type conversion), the alpha ones added or deleted
trailing spaces. 

Memory addresses were decimal. Every nybble was addressable. An unsigned packed one digit
variable had a length of one nybble. 

The Cobol compiler allowed inline assembly code, which could reference Cobol paragraph and
data names, including qualification. Assembler code was seldom required; you could do
almost everything in Cobol. Major chunks of the operating system (MCP) were written in
Cobol. 

Executable programs were typically one third the size of the same program compiled on IBM
or most any other computer. 

There were three instruction sets available, two of which could be resident
sumultaneously. The other two were FORTRAN, which had lots of floating point instructions,
and ALGOL, which was used principally by the OS kernel and compilers. The job scheduler
treated the instruction set as a required resource. It would not schedule a Cobol and
FORTRAN program to run at the same time. 

The most well-known models were the B2000, B3500 and B4700.

Development stopped when Burroughs was swollowed by Unisys in 1986.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

- **From:** "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net>

- **Message-ID:** `<8pTgk.6288$CY7.454@newsfe06.iad>`

Fujitsu V3 is free and can be distributed, but make certain that you DO read 
what it says at the web page Pete mentioned, i.e.

"For those of you who are learning COBOL on your own or taking a COBOL class and 
need a COBOL compiler to experiment with - but NOT create production 
applications - we provide Fujitsu COBOL Version 3 for FREE. Note that this is a 
back-level version of Fujitsu COBOL. It provides support for ANSI Standard 
COBOL, many Fujitsu COBOL extensions, is fully Windows compatible, and contains 
a complete set of softcopy manuals. It is not a product we actively support and 
as such it is not viable for creating production applications; however, it is 
fully stable and excellent for most COBOL exercises"
```

<a id="t-32dd5e5f68"></a>
## Thread: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s — 1 msgs

### Re: Old broken COBOL programs from the 70s and 80s

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2008-07-19T11:32:00-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4881D0B0.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
>>> On 7/18/2008 at 1:19 PM, in message
<de34a168-288d-4a51-81aa-5fb754e737e0@k13g2000hse.googlegroups.com>,
x01001x<xemail@softhome.net> wrote:

>> >Why were companies still using such ancient code in 1999?
>> >What kind of hardware would even still be heavily used at that time
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> What kind of companies would be using these utilities?

Banking deposit and loans systems, in our case.

Billing systems and other financial systems in other cases.

These things simply don't differ much as the years go by.

We are constantly adding new features as required by the business.  But the
old things still happen.  If you make a deposit we add it to your balance. 
Make a withdrawal and we subtract it.  Bounce a check and we charge you an
enormous fee.  :-)  What possible reason would we have to replace this?  In
the mid-80s (before my time!) we converted our 'databases' from VSAM
(indexed files) to DL/I.  The programs themselves, though, other than the
data access routines didn't really change (fom what I'm told).

In the future we may convert to DB2.  In this case I imagine more of a
rewrite than the previous, in order to take advantage of SQL and perhaps
having business logic at the database level.  But I am betting our 'deposit
system posting program' will still exist, and still be in Cobol.

By the way, we've had 8-digit dates in our databases since at least the mid
80s.  Perhaps it was done during the VSAM -> DL/I conversion.  Perhaps it
was "always" that way.

Frank
```

```
On Sun, 20 Jul 2008 20:46:09 -0600, Louis Krupp <lkrupp@pssw.nospam.com.invalid> wrote:

>Robert wrote:
>> On Sun, 20 Jul 2008 15:33:14 -0500, "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote:
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
>Medium Systems were designed for COBOL and not much else.

I worked on Medium Systems, not Small. There was a FORTRAN instruction set, but I think
few Medium Systems were used for scientific work. Threre was an RPG compiler that seemed
intended for Small Systems. 

Medium Systems were thought to be 'good for banking,' whatever that means. I guess it was
all that emphasis on decimal arithmetic.  Large Systems, which were derived from General
Electric designs and ran GECOS, were used by government and large companies. 

>> Development stopped when Burroughs was swollowed by Unisys in 1986.
>
>Unisys was formed by the merger of Burroughs and Sperry.  A high school 
>friend who worked on the merger said much later that it was like tying 
>two bricks together to make them fly better. 

The mainframe market began a long decline in the early 80s.  Other US mainframe makers
went out of business. Unisys stopped trying to compete with IBM on hardware, switched to
selling services. Their revenues are now 80% services and 20% mainframe.

> I believe that Sperry's 
>headquarters in Roseville, PA is now Unisys HQ.  Burroughs Intergalactic 
>Headquarters in Detroit is but a memory.

Blue Bell, PA.
```

Hi,

It's been awhile that my Cobol program (that accesses MS SQL v8.0)
can't perfectly read the 'entire' records on selected range. The code
below

            EXEC SQL
             DECLARE CSR20 CURSOR FOR SELECT DISTINCT
                    A.RECCNT
                   ,A.TERMNO
                   ,A.OPERNO
                   ,A.TRANNO
                   ,A.PRODNO
                   ,A.ITEM_DESC
                   ,A.DEPT
                   ,A.VENDOR
                   ,A.RECCODE
                   ,A.QTY

              WHERE ( A.DTE = :SL-DTE )
            END-EXEC

works sometime.... but on some instances, it is not reading "all" the

selected range option is about 350 records. Is anyone here experience

            EXEC SQL OPEN CSR20 END-EXEC
